الفصل الثالث في كاية شبهات البنكرين الموجودات الروحانية والجواب عنها

زعم أكثر المتكلمين : أن إثبات موجود ممكن الوجود ، لا يكون متحيزاً ، ولا حالا في المتحيز : محال . واحتجوا عليه بوجوه :
الحجة الأولى : قالوا : ثبت بالدليل : أن إله العالم يجب أن لا يكون متحيزاً ، ولا حالاً في المتحيز . فلو فرضنا موجوداً آخر بهذه الصفة ، لكان الموجود : مساوياً لذات الله تعالى في أنه غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز . والإستواء في هذا الحكم ، يوجب الإستواء في الماهية . بدليل أنا إذا أردنا أن نذكر في ذات الله تعالى : صفة ، بإعتبارها تمتاز ذاته عن سائر الذوات ، لم نقدر إلا على ذكر هذه الصفة. وهي : أنه ذات ليست بمتحيزة ، ولا حالة في شيء من المتحيزات . والصفة إذا كانت كاشفة عن الحقيقة ، كان الإشتراك فيها ، يوجب الإشتراك في الحقيقة . فثبت : أنه لو حصل موجود بهذه الصفة ، لكان ذلك الموجود مثيلا لذات الله تعالى . والمثلان يجب استواؤ هما في جميع اللوازم . فيلزم : إما القول يكون الكل : آلهة واجبة الوجود ، أو بكون الكل عبيداً ممكنة الوجود . ولما كان الكل محالاً . ثبت : أن إثبات موجود بهذه الصفة . محال .

الحجة الثانية لهم : قالوا : الحس دل على وجود المتحيز ، وعلى وجود الصفات القائمة به . وأما القسم الثالث ، فلا يمكن إثباته ، إلا لأجل افتقار أحد هذين القسمين إليه . لأن ما لا يكون العلم بوجوده بديهياً ، لم يجز المصير إلى إثباته ، إلا إذا قضى العقل بإحتياج ما علم وجوده إليه . إلا أنا لما اعترفنا بأن الإله تعالى غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز ، كان وجوده كافياً في وجود هذه المتحيزات ، وفي وجود الأعراض القائمة بها . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم يبق على وجود موجود آخر غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز : دليل . وما لا دليل عليه أصلاً ، كان القول بإثباته موجباً للجهالات .

الحجة الثالثة : وهي مختصة بنفي العقول والنفوس - قالوا : هذه العقول والنفوس التي تثبتها الفلاسفة . يزعمون : أنها قديمة فهي غير متحيزة عن ذات الله تعالى ، لا بالزمان ولا بالمكان ، ولا بالوجود والعدم . لأن التقدير تقدير أنها قديمة . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع امتياز بعضها عن بعض في نفس الأمر ، وهذا يقتضي اتحاد الإثنين ، وهو محال .

واعلم : أن هذه الوجوه ضعيفة :
أما الحجة الأولى : فالجواب عنها : أن المساواة في كونه غير متحيز ، ولا حال في المتحيز : مساواة في الصفة السلبية ، لا توجب المساواة في تمام الماهية . والدليل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إن كل حقيقتين ، فلا بد وأن يشتركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنهما ، وذلك برهان قاطع على أن المساواة في السلوب ، لا توجب المساواة في تمام الماهية .
الثاني : إن كل نوعين داخلين تحت جنس واحد ، فهما مشتركان في طبيعة ذلك الجنس ، ولم يلزم من حصول تلك المشاركة ، حصول المماثلة المطلقة .
Chapter Three: On repelling the doubts of the deniers of spiritual beings and the reply to them.

Most theologians claimed that affirming an existent which is possible of existence, that is neither a body (mutahayyiz) nor a state (hal) in a body, is impossible. And they argued for it by [several] proofs:

The first proof: They said: It has been established by evidence that the God of the world must not be a body nor a state in a body. So if we suppose another existent with this attribute, that existent would be equal to the essence of God Almighty in that it is not a body nor a state in a body. And equality in this ruling necessitates equality in quiddity (mahiyya). The evidence for this is that if we wish to mention an attribute of the essence of God Almighty by which His essence is distinguished from all other essences, we are only able to mention this attribute: namely, that He is an essence not being a body nor a state in anything of bodies. And if the attribute is disclosing of the reality, then sharing in it necessitates sharing in the reality. So it is established that if an existent with this attribute were to occur, that existent would be a likeness (mithl) of the essence of God Almighty. And two likenesses must be equal in all concomitants (lawaazim). So it follows either that all are gods, necessarily existent, or that all are servants, possibly existent. And since both are impossible, it is established that affirming an existent with this attribute is impossible.

The second proof for them: They said: Sense perception indicates the existence of the body and the existence of the accidents inhering in it. As for the third category, it cannot be affirmed except due to the need of one of these two categories for it. Because that whose existence is not known intuitively cannot be affirmed unless reason judges that what is known to exist needs it. But since we have acknowledged that God Almighty is not a body nor a state in a body, His existence is sufficient for the existence of these bodies and for the existence of the accidents inhering in them. And if that is so, there remains no evidence for the existence of another existent that is neither a body nor a state in a body. And that for which there is no evidence at all, affirming it necessitates ignorant things.

The third proof: And it is specific to the denial of intellects and souls. They said: These intellects and souls that the philosophers affirm – they claim that they are eternal – so they are not separated from the essence of God Almighty, neither by time nor by place nor by existence and non-existence. Because the assumption is that they are eternal. And if that is so, it is impossible for some of them to be distinct from others in themselves, and this necessitates the unity of the two, which is impossible.

And know that these proofs are weak:
As for the first proof, the reply to it is that equality in being not a body and not a state in a body is equality in a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya), and does not necessitate equality in the complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyya). And the evidence for it has [several] aspects:
The first: Every two realities must necessarily share in the negation of everything other than them from both of them, and that is a decisive proof that equality in negations does not necessitate equality in the complete quiddity.
The second: Every two species falling under one genus share in the nature of that genus, and from the occurrence of that sharing, the occurrence of absolute resemblance (al-mumathala al-mutlaqa) is not necessary.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of three arguments by the majority of theologians (أكثر المتكلمين) purporting to show that it is impossible to affirm the existence of a contingent being that is neither a body (متحيز) nor inhering in a body, and then a critical refutation of the first argument. The author likely intends to refute all three, but only the refutation of the first is given here.

The first argument runs as follows: It is proven that God is not a body and not an accident. If another being had the same attribute (non-bodily, non-inhering), then it would be equal to God in that distinguishing characteristic. But equality in the distinguishing attribute implies equality in essence (ماهية), because the attribute reveals the reality. Thus the other being would be a likeness (مثل) of God. Two likenesses must be equal in all concomitants; therefore either both are necessary existents (آلهة واجبة الوجود) or both are contingent servants (عبيد ممكنة الوجود). Both possibilities are false, so such a being cannot exist.

The second argument relies on epistemology: Only bodies and accidents are known by sense. A third category can only be posited if reason needs it to explain the known. But God's existence already accounts for the existence of all bodies and accidents, so there is no need for any other non-bodily being. Without evidence, affirmation leads to ignorance.

The third argument targets the philosophers' eternal intellects and souls. If they are eternal and not separated from God by time, place, or existence/non-existence, then they cannot be distinguished from one another or from God, leading to identity (اتحاد) — an impossibility.

After presenting these, the author declares them weak and proceeds to refute the first. The refutation points out that equality in negative attributes (الصفة السلبية) — like 'not a body' — does not imply equality in the complete quiddity. Two realities always share in the negation of everything else, yet they are distinct. Similarly, two species under one genus share the generic nature but are not absolutely identical. Thus the first argument's inference from shared negative attribute to essential likeness is invalid.

Key Terms
متحيز
ماهية
صفة سلبية
مثل
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sharing a negative attribute (like 'not being a body') with God implies being essentially like God.
Correction: Negative attributes are not peculiar to God alone; many things share the same negations (e.g., angels, souls). Sharing a negation does not determine the positive essence, so it does not entail similarity in quiddity.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation claim that equality in negative attributes does not necessitate equality in complete quiddity?
A) Because negative attributes are not real attributes.
B) Because every two realities share the negation of everything else, yet they are distinct.
C) Because God's essence is unique and cannot be known.
D) Because the theologians have not proven that the attribute is distinguishing.

Key Takeaway
Shared negative attributes do not prove shared essence; the first argument fails because it mistakes a common negative attribute for a defining characteristic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there people in your life whom you judge as 'just like you' because they share a negative trait (e.g., not being wealthy, not having a degree)? How does this lesson warn you against reducing someone's entire essence to a shared negation? What step will you take this week to see others more fully?
?
This argument tried to limit God by equating Him with a contingent being simply because both are not bodies. When in your prayer or thoughts do you inadvertently compare God to creation, limiting His majesty? How can you actively replace those comparisons with awe based on His positive attributes?
?
Imagine you defended Allah's attributes by solely listing what He is not. According to this lesson, why is that insufficient for knowing His essence? What is one positive affirmation about Allah you will meditate on today to deepen your ma'rifah?

Allah's Existence
beginner

The Second and Third Arguments
Second and third arguments from the theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument (based on sufficiency of God) and the third argument (against multiple eternal non-bodily beings) that theologians used to deny non-bodily contingent existents.

Core Idea
If God suffices for creation, why postulate other non-bodily beings? And multiple eternal beings would collapse into identity.

The theologians present two more arguments. The second argues that since God's existence already explains all bodies and accidents, there is no need for other non-bodily beings. The third, targeting philosophers' eternal intellects and souls, argues that multiple eternal non-bodily beings cannot be distinct, leading to identity. This lesson examines both.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the second argument which relies on the principle that only sense-perceptible beings (bodies and accidents) are known directly, and other beings are only posited if necessary. Since God's existence already accounts for all bodies and accidents, there is no need for another non-bodily being.
This argument is epistemological: no evidence means no affirmation.
2
Read the third argument
Read the third argument specifically aimed at the philosophers' eternal intellects and souls. It argues that if they are eternal and not separated from God by time, place, or existence, then they cannot be distinct from each other or from God, leading to the impossibility of unity (ittihad).
This argument focuses on the impossibility of multiplicity among eternal beings.
3
Reflect on the overall structure
Consider that these arguments are presented as a cumulative case. Note that the author will later declare them weak, but here they are presented without refutation.
The refutation of these arguments may appear in subsequent lessons.

On what basis does the second argument deny the need for another non-bodily existent?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Third Argument: Against Separate Intellects and Souls
Presents a philosophical argument against separate intellects and souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale behind the argument that multiple eternal, non-material intellects and souls lead to an absurd unity.

Core Idea
Multiple eternal non-material beings cannot be distinct; they must be one.

This lesson presents a crisp logical argument against the philosophers' claim that there exist multiple separate intellects and souls. By examining the implications of their being eternal and non-material, we see that such multiplicity is rationally impossible. Let's work through this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise, logical structure.
The passage uses a syllogistic style.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers claim separate intellects and souls are eternal and non-material. Understand what 'eternal' and 'non-material' mean in this context.
Eternal here means without beginning.
3
Analyze the impossibility of distinction
If they are all eternal and non-material, they cannot be distinguished in time, place, existence, or non-existence. Thus, they cannot be distinct in reality.
4
Draw the conclusion
The only logical outcome is that they would all be identical, leading to a unity that contradicts the claimed multiplicity, which is absurd (muhal).
This refutes the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : وهي مختصة بنفي العقول والنفوس - قالوا : هذه العقول والنفوس التي تثبتها الفلاسفة . يزعمون : أنها قديمة [ فهي غير متحيزة عن ذات الله تعالى ، لا بالزمان ولا بالمكان ، ولا بالوجود والعدم . لأن التقدير تقدير أنها قديمة (۲) ) وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع امتياز بعضها عن بعض في نفس الأمر ، وهذا يقتضي اتحاد الإثنين ، وهو محال
The third argument: it is specific to negating the intellects and souls. They said: These intellects and souls that the philosophers affirm. They claim that they are eternal, [so they are non-extended away from the essence of God Most High, neither in time nor in place, nor in existence nor non-existence. Because the estimation is that they are eternal (2). And if that is the case, it is impossible for some of them to be distinct from others in reality, and this necessitates the unity of the two, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument, specifically directed against the existence of separate intellects and souls (al-ʿuqūl wa al-nufūs) as posited by the philosophers (al-falāsifa). The argument proceeds: The philosophers claim that these intellects and souls are eternal and non-material. Being eternal, they are not distinct from God's essence in time, place, existence, or non-existence. If they are all co-eternal and non-material, then they cannot be differentiated from one another in reality. Hence, they would all be identical to God (or each other), leading to a unity that is contradictory to their claimed multiplicity. The conclusion is that such multiplicity is impossible, and therefore the existence of separate intellects and souls is invalid.

امتياز
اتحاد الإثنين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies all immaterial beings, including God.
Correction: The argument specifically targets the philosophers' claim of multiple eternal separate intellects and souls, not the existence of God or all immaterial beings.

Checkpoint

B) The philosophers' claim leads to an impossible unity.
C) God is extended.
D) Intellects are created in time.

Key Takeaway
Multiple eternal non-material beings cannot be distinct; therefore, the philosophers' separate intellects and souls are rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument reveals the absurdity (muhal) of multiplicity in the eternal. In your daily life, where do you see a tendency to 'multiply' the ultimate source of meaning or security? What would it mean to live out the unity of the Eternal?

Fifth Proof: Indivisibility of the Accident of Existence
Fifth proof against the divisibility of the accident of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the accident of existence cannot be divided, even if its locus is divisible.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be divided; its parts would either equal the whole or be non-existent, both impossible.

This proof tackles a subtle question: can the attribute of existence, which ‘lives’ in a divisible body, itself be divided? The author argues by contradiction, showing that assuming divisibility leads to absurd consequences. Follow the logical steps to see why existence must remain a unified whole.

Guided Path
1
State the assumption
Read the opening premise: existence is an accident attaching to essences. Then notice the conditional: if the accident divides when the subject divides, then existence would be divided.
This is the assumption to be refuted.
2
First impossibility
If a part of existence is itself existence, then the part would be equal to the whole—a logical impossibility.
A part cannot be the whole.
3
Second impossibility (first reason)
If the parts are not existence, when they combine, either no extra form arises (absurd) or an extra form arises that itself must be existence or not—leading to a regress.
Focus on the paradox of composition.
4
Second impossibility (second reason)
If parts are not existence, then each part of a body would lack existence, making the body non-existent—contradicting the body’s existence.
5
Conclude the proof
The only way out is that the accident does not divide; divisibility of the locus does not force divisibility of the accident.
This is the desired result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : الوجود صفة عارضة للماهيات ، فإذا أشرنا إلى جسم معين ، وقلنا : إنه موجود ، فيجب أن يكون الوجود صفة قائمة به فلو لزم انقسام الحال لإنقسام المحل ، لزم أن يكون ذلك الوجود منقسماً وذلك محال . لأن كل واحد من أجزاء الوجود . إن كان وجوداً ، لزم كون الجزء مساوياً للكل ، وهو محال . وإن كان كل واحد من أجزاء الوجود ليس بوجود ، فهذا محال لوجهين الأول : إن تلك الأجزاء عند اجتماعها ، إن لم تحدث هناك هيئة زائدة ، لزم أن يكون الوجود حاصلاً ، وإن حصلت هيئة زائدة ، فتلك الهيئة الزائدة ، إما أن يكون وجوداً ، وإما أن لا يكون . وإن لم ينقسم ، فحينئذ لم يلزم من انقسام المحل انقسام الحال . وهو المطلوب الثاني : إن كل واحد من أجزاء الوجود، إذا لم يكن وجوداً ، لزم أن يقال : كل واحد من أجزاء الجسم المنقسم ، فهو غير موصوف بالوجود ، فوجب أن تكون تلك الأجزاء غير موجودة ، فجزء الجسم الموجود يجب أن لا يكون موجوداً . وذلك محال
The fifth proof: Existence (al-wujud) is an accident that attaches to essences (mahiyyat). So when we point to a specific body and say 'it exists', existence must be an attribute subsisting in it. So if the accident were to divide due to the division of its locus, then that existence would be divided, and that is impossible. Because each one of the parts of existence: if it is existence, then the part would equal the whole, and that is impossible. And if each one of the parts of existence is not existence, then this is impossible for two reasons: First: those parts, when they are combined, if no additional configuration (hay'a za'ida) arises, then existence would have occurred; and if an additional configuration occurs, then that additional configuration is either existence or not. And if it does not divide, then the division of the locus does not necessitate the division of the accident, which is the desired result. Second: if each one of the parts of existence is not existence, then it must be said that each one of the parts of the divided body is not described by existence, so it follows that those parts are non-existent. Then a part of an existing body must not exist, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is a proof against the divisibility of the accident of existence. The author argues that if existence were divisible along with its subject, then its parts would either be existence or not. If a part is existence, then that part is as complete as the whole, which is impossible (a part cannot be the whole). If the parts are not existence, then two absurdities result: either the combination of non-existence parts somehow yields existence without an extra form, or an extra form arises which itself would need to be existence or not; the only way out is if the accident does not divide, which is the claim. The second reason is that if the parts are not existence, then each part of the body would not possess existence, meaning each part is non-existent, contradicting the body's existence.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
عرض
هيئة زائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since the body is divisible, its attribute of existence must also be divisible.
Correction: The proof shows this leads to absurdities; the accident does not necessarily divide even if the locus divides.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given against the parts of existence being non-existent?
A) The parts would combine to form existence only if a new form arises
B) The parts must each be existence
C) The body would be non-existent
D) The whole would be greater than the sum

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be divided because assuming divisibility leads to logical impossibilities; the accident remains whole even if its subject is divided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is an indivisible gift from Allah. Do you live as if your existence is whole and unified for His sake, or do you fragment your life into separate spheres—some for Him, some for yourself? What one fragment of your life can you reunite under His command today?
?
The proof shows that if existence were divided, parts would either be equal to the whole or nonexistent. In your own life, do you treat certain sins or habits as small, insignificant parts of your existence that do not affect the whole? How does the indivisible nature of your existence challenge you to see every moment as fully accountable before Allah?
?
If existence cannot be divided, then your very being is a single, undivided act of Allah's creation. How often do you act as if you own parts of your existence independently? Write down one area where you are tempted to claim ownership, and commit to acknowledging Allah's sole possession of it in your next prayer.

The Eighth Argument: Dichotomy of Contingency and Necessity
Begins the eighth argument with a dichotomy on divisibility of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dichotomy that the eighth argument imposes on the accidental attributes of contingency and necessity-by-another.

Core Idea
Accidental attributes force a decisive division-or-no-division choice.

The eighth argument builds on the theme that attributes like contingency and necessity-by-another are accidental, not essential. The mutakallim sets up a dichotomy: either these concepts divide with their substrate (the body) or they do not. Either way, the opponent's position is undermined. This passage presents the premises; although the conclusion is cut off, the logical structure is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two claims: bodies are contingent in themselves and necessary by another. Then note that these are accidental attributes.
The passage breaks off at 'if' – don't worry about the missing conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument first states that bodies are contingent in themselves and necessary by another. Second, it says these attributes are accidental, external to the essence.
3
Understand the dichotomy
The argument then asks: do these concepts (contingency and necessity-by-another) divide with the division of their substrate (the body) or not? If they do not divide, the opponent's claim fails. If they do divide, each part would be... (the text cuts off).
Think about why dividing or not dividing each leads to a problem for the opponent.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument shows how a mutakallim uses logical division to force a conclusion. The accidental nature of the attributes makes them subject to such analysis.
This ties into the larger proof for a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : لا شك أن هذه الأجسام ممكنة بذواتها ، واجبة بغيرها . فكونها ممكنة بذواتها ، واجبة بغيرها صفات عرضية خارجة عن الماهية . فنقول : هذه المفهومات إن لم تنقسم الانقسام محلها ، فقد بطل الكلام المذكور، وإن انقسمت فكل واحد من أجزاء الإمكان، ومن أجزاء الوجوب بالغير ، إن
The eighth argument: There is no doubt that these bodies are contingent in themselves and necessary by another. So their being contingent in themselves and necessary by another are accidental attributes external to the essence. We say: These concepts, if they do not divide with the division of their substrate, then the mentioned discourse is invalid; and if they divide, then each one of the parts of possibility and of the parts of necessity by another, if...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage begins the eighth argument. The author states that bodies are contingent in themselves (ممكنة بذواتها) and necessary by another (واجبة بغيرها). The properties of contingency and necessity-by-another are accidental attributes (صفات عرضية) that are external to the essence (خارجة عن الماهية). The argument then sets up a dichotomy: these concepts (المفهومات), i.e., contingency and necessity, either (1) do not divide with the division of their substrate (the body), in which case the opponent's claim that accidents divide is false; or (2) they do divide. The passage breaks off at 'if' (إن), but the intended conclusion is likely that if they divide, each part would be either the attribute or not, leading to contradiction. The argument is incomplete here, but the logic is consistent with previous arguments.

Correction: The passage explicitly states they are accidental attributes (صفات عرضية) external to the essence.

?
The argument forces a logical dichotomy: either the concepts divide or they don't. The Soul is Not a Body: Self-Awareness Argument
Answer to first objection: proving soul is not a body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the human soul cannot be a body because one can be aware of oneself without any awareness of occupying space.

Core Idea
Self-awareness proves the soul is not a body.

This lesson presents a key argument from the author that the human self is not a material body. By examining the phenomenon of self-awareness in moments of deep concentration, we see that we know our own essence without any awareness of being a physical object. This leads to the conclusion that the soul is an abstract substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Note how the author uses a conditional argument and a real-world example.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key premise
The author first recalls a principle from a previous chapter: a body cannot be an attribute in another. Then he applies it to the human self. Identify the two possibilities: either the self is a body or not.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author gives an example of a person deeply absorbed in thought. Notice how this example shows that self-knowledge occurs without awareness of being a body. Why does this refute the idea that the self is a body?
Consider the logical necessity: if the self were a body, knowing it would require knowing it as a body.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the self must be an abstract substance. Reflect on why this conclusion follows from the premises.
This argument is foundational for the rest of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : إن الدليل الذي ذكرتم قائم في جميع الحيوانات ، فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا لها نفوساً ناطقة مجردة . وذلك بعيد . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : إنا قد دللنا في باب « نفي الهيولى » ، أن كون الجسم جسماً متحيزاً ، لا يمكن أن يكون صفة قائمة بمحل آخر ، بل هو ذات قائمة بنفسها ، مستقلة بذاتها. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ذلك الإنسان إما أن يكون متحيزاً وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . فإن كانت ذاته جوهراً متحيزاً وجب أن يكون العالم بتلك الذات : عالماً بالمتحيز ، وإلا لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد معلوماً مجهولاً معاً . وهو محال . لكنه لا يجب ذلك ، بدليل : أنه إذا كان شديد الاهتمام بأمر من الأمور ، فإنه في تلك الساعة [ قد يقول : قلت كذا ، وفعلت كذا . فهو في تلك الساعة عالم بذاته المخصوصة ، مع أنه في تلك الساعة (۳) ] يكون غافلاً عن معنى التحيز الذاهب في الجهات الستة . فثبت : أنه لو كانت ذاته متحيزة ، لكان كل من عرف ذاته ، وجب أن يكون عارفاً بالمتحيز ، وثبت : أنه قد يعرف ذاته ، حال ما يكون غافلاً عن المتحيز ، فوجب أن لا تكون ذاته متحيزة. وإذا لم تكن ذاته متحيزة ، وجب أن تكون ذاته (1) جوهراً مجرداً ، وهو المطلوب . وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : ( إن هذا الكلام بعينه يدل على أن ذاته أن تكون جوهراً مجرداً ) فنقول : هذا غير لازم ، لأن الجوهر المجرد ذات مخصوصة ، وكونه ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز : قيد سلبي . وهذا القيد السلبي مغاير لتلك الذات المخصوصة ، وإذا حصلت هذه المغايرة ، ثبت : أنه لا يمتنع كون تلك الذات المخصوصة : معلومة ، مع الذهول عن ذلك القيد السلبي ، بخلاف ما إذا كانت ذاته المخصوصة متحيزة ، لأنه بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، كان تحيزه نفس تلك الذات، لا أمراً مغايراً لها ، فكان يجب أن يمتنع كونه عالماً بتلك الذات المخصوصة مع الذهول عن التحيز . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، ثبت أن تلك الذات المخصوصة يمتنع كونها متحيزة ، فظهر الفرق بين البابين . وأما السؤال الثالث فجوابه : إنه لو ثبت أن الحيوانات تدرك أنفسها المخصوصة ، حال كونها غافلة عن حقيقة التحيز ، لكان السؤال لازماً ، إلا أنه لا سبيل إلى معرفة هذه المقدمة . ثم بتقدير أن نثبت لها نفوساً مجردة ، إلا أنه لا يجب كونها نفوساً ناطقة . لأن التجرد عن المادة : قيد سلبي ، والاشتراك في القيود السلبية لا يدل على التساوي في تمام الماهية
The third question: Indeed the proof you mentioned applies to all animals, so it would require you to affirm for them rational abstract souls. That is unlikely. And the answer to the first question: We say: We have demonstrated in the chapter 'Negation of Hyle' that the body being a body occupying space cannot be an attribute subsisting in another substrate, but rather it is a self-subsisting essence, independent in itself. When this is established, we say: that human is either occupying space or not. If his essence is a substance occupying space, then it would be necessary that the knower of that essence knows the occupant of space; otherwise the same thing would be both known and unknown simultaneously, which is impossible. But this is not necessary, as evidenced by the fact that when he is deeply absorbed in some matter, at that moment he states: 'I said such and such, and did such and such.' So at that moment he is aware of his particular self, while at that moment he is oblivious to the meaning of occupying space extending in the six directions. So it is established: if his essence were occupied with space, then everyone who knows his essence would necessarily know the occupant of space; and it is established that he knows his essence while being oblivious to the occupant of space; so it is necessary that his essence is not occupied with space. And if his essence is not occupied with space, then his essence must be an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad), which is what is sought. As for the second question: namely his statement 'This very same argument indicates that his essence is an abstract substance' – we say: this is not necessary, because the abstract substance is a particular essence, and its not being occupying space nor inherence in an occupant of space is a negative attribute. This negative attribute is distinct from that particular essence. When this distinctness is established, it is established that it is not impossible for that particular essence to be known while being heedless of that negative attribute, unlike the case if his particular essence were occupying space, because assuming that were the case, its occupying space would be that very essence itself, not something distinct from it; so it would be impossible for him to know that particular essence while being heedless of occupying space. And since it is not so, it is established that that particular essence cannot be occupying space; thus the difference between the two cases becomes clear. As for the third question, its answer: If it were established that animals perceive their particular selves while being oblivious to the reality of occupying space, then the question would be necessary; however, there is no way to know this premise. Then, supposing we affirm for them abstract souls, yet they need not be rational souls, because abstraction from matter is a negative attribute, and sharing in negative attributes does not imply equality in the complete quiddity.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents three objections and their answers concerning the argument that the soul is an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad). The first objection is not explicitly stated here but likely claimed that the proof applies to all objects, not just humans. The author begins by answering the first objection: he revisits the proof from the chapter on the negation of hyle (prime matter) that a body occupying space cannot be an attribute in another; it is a self-subsisting entity. He then applies this: the human self either occupies space or not. If it did, then knowing the self would require knowing it as an occupant of space, but one can be aware of one's self while entirely unaware of being an occupant of space (e.g., when intensely focused). Thus the self cannot be a body, so it must be an abstract substance. The second objection argues that the same reasoning could show that the abstract substance itself is not known because one might be unaware of its negative attribute (not being a body). The author distinguishes: the negative attribute (not being an occupant) is distinct from the essence. So knowing the essence without the attribute is possible, unlike the case where the essence is identical to the attribute (as with a body where occupying space is the essence itself). The third objection concerns animals: if the proof works for humans, it should also work for animals, implying they have rational souls. The author replies that we do not know if animals have self-awareness of this kind; even if they do, they could have abstract souls without rationality, because abstraction is a negative attribute shared by many, but rationality is a positive quiddity not implied by mere abstraction.

Key Terms
الجوهر المجرد
المتحيز
النفس الناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that the human self is a body because we identify with our physical form.
Correction: The author shows that self-awareness occurs without any awareness of being a body, proving the self is not a body.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that if the self were a body (occupying space), then knowing oneself would require knowing oneself as a space-occupier?
A) Because the self and the body are two different things.
B) Because the self would be identical with the attribute of occupying space, so knowing one requires knowing the other.
C) Because the body is known through the senses.
D) Because the self is not directly known.

Key Takeaway
The human self is not a body because one can be aware of oneself while completely unaware of being an occupant of space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you are deeply absorbed in a task, you are aware of your 'I' without thinking about your body. Does this ever make you feel a separation between your true self and your physical form? How does that change your understanding of who you really are?
?
If your self is not your body, then your worth does not come from your appearance, health, or physical abilities. Are you living as if your identity is tied to your body? What would change if you truly internalized that your self is an abstract substance that will outlive your body?
?
The argument shows that self-awareness does not require awareness of being a body. How often do you actually reflect on your own self as a non-physical entity? What distractions in your life prevent you from experiencing that deeper self-awareness in a way that draws you closer to Allah?

Negative Attributes vs. Essence: Second Objection Answered
Answer to second objection: distinguishing negative attribute from essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the negative attribute 'not a body' is distinct from the essence, and how this answers the objection that the soul cannot be known when unaware of its abstraction.

Core Idea
Negative attributes are distinct from essence, allowing knowledge of essence without knowledge of the negative attribute.

The second objection claims that the same argument used to prove the soul is not a body would also prove that the abstract soul cannot be known when one is unaware of its abstraction. The author replies by distinguishing between the essence itself and its negative attributes. This lesson clarifies that distinction and shows why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First read the objection as stated: the same argument would indicate that the soul's essence is an abstract substance. Then read the author's answer carefully.
The objection is subtle; focus on the distinction between essence and attribute.
2
Understand the distinction
The author says the abstract substance is a particular essence, and its not being a body is a negative attribute (qayd salbi). This negative attribute is distinct from the essence. Identify why this distinction matters.
Contrast this with the case of a body, where being a body is identical with the essence.
3
Apply the distinction to knowledge
Because the negative attribute is distinct, one can know the essence without knowing the attribute. In the case of a body, the attribute is identical, so knowledge requires knowing both. See how this resolves the objection.
4
Reflect on the difference
The author concludes that the two cases are different. Ensure you understand why the second objection does not undermine the first argument.
This shows the importance of analyzing attributes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : إن الدليل الذي ذكرتم قائم في جميع الحيوانات ، فيلزمكم أن تثبتوا لها نفوساً ناطقة مجردة . وذلك بعيد . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : إنا قد دللنا في باب « نفي الهيولى » ، أن كون الجسم جسماً متحيزاً ، لا يمكن أن يكون صفة قائمة بمحل آخر ، بل هو ذات قائمة بنفسها ، مستقلة بذاتها. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ذلك الإنسان إما أن يكون متحيزاً وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . فإن كانت ذاته جوهراً متحيزاً وجب أن يكون العالم بتلك الذات : عالماً بالمتحيز ، وإلا لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد معلوماً مجهولاً معاً . وهو محال . لكنه لا يجب ذلك ، بدليل : أنه إذا كان شديد الاهتمام بأمر من الأمور ، فإنه في تلك الساعة [ قد يقول : قلت كذا ، وفعلت كذا . فهو في تلك الساعة عالم بذاته المخصوصة ، مع أنه في تلك الساعة (۳) ] يكون غافلاً عن معنى التحيز الذاهب في الجهات الستة . فثبت : أنه لو كانت ذاته متحيزة ، لكان كل من عرف ذاته ، وجب أن يكون عارفاً بالمتحيز ، وثبت : أنه قد يعرف ذاته ، حال ما يكون غافلاً عن المتحيز ، فوجب أن لا تكون ذاته متحيزة. وإذا لم تكن ذاته متحيزة ، وجب أن تكون ذاته (1) جوهراً مجرداً ، وهو المطلوب . وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : ( إن هذا الكلام بعينه يدل على أن ذاته أن تكون جوهراً مجرداً ) فنقول : هذا غير لازم ، لأن الجوهر المجرد ذات مخصوصة ، وكونه ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز : قيد سلبي . وهذا القيد السلبي مغاير لتلك الذات المخصوصة ، وإذا حصلت هذه المغايرة ، ثبت : أنه لا يمتنع كون تلك الذات المخصوصة : معلومة ، مع الذهول عن ذلك القيد السلبي ، بخلاف ما إذا كانت ذاته المخصوصة متحيزة ، لأنه بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، كان تحيزه نفس تلك الذات، لا أمراً مغايراً لها ، فكان يجب أن يمتنع كونه عالماً بتلك الذات المخصوصة مع الذهول عن التحيز . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، ثبت أن تلك الذات المخصوصة يمتنع كونها متحيزة ، فظهر الفرق بين البابين . وأما السؤال الثالث فجوابه : إنه لو ثبت أن الحيوانات تدرك أنفسها المخصوصة ، حال كونها غافلة عن حقيقة التحيز ، لكان السؤال لازماً ، إلا أنه لا سبيل إلى معرفة هذه المقدمة . ثم بتقدير أن نثبت لها نفوساً مجردة ، إلا أنه لا يجب كونها نفوساً ناطقة . لأن التجرد عن المادة : قيد سلبي ، والاشتراك في القيود السلبية لا يدل على التساوي في تمام الماهية
The third question: Indeed the proof you mentioned applies to all animals, so it would require you to affirm for them rational abstract souls. That is unlikely. And the answer to the first question: We say: We have demonstrated in the chapter 'Negation of Hyle' that the body being a body occupying space cannot be an attribute subsisting in another substrate, but rather it is a self-subsisting essence, independent in itself. When this is established, we say: that human is either occupying space or not. If his essence is a substance occupying space, then it would be necessary that the knower of that essence knows the occupant of space; otherwise the same thing would be both known and unknown simultaneously, which is impossible. But this is not necessary, as evidenced by the fact that when he is deeply absorbed in some matter, at that moment he states: 'I said such and such, and did such and such.' So at that moment he is aware of his particular self, while at that moment he is oblivious to the meaning of occupying space extending in the six directions. So it is established: if his essence were occupied with space, then everyone who knows his essence would necessarily know the occupant of space; and it is established that he knows his essence while being oblivious to the occupant of space; so it is necessary that his essence is not occupied with space. And if his essence is not occupied with space, then his essence must be an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad), which is what is sought. As for the second question: namely his statement 'This very same argument indicates that his essence is an abstract substance' – we say: this is not necessary, because the abstract substance is a particular essence, and its not being occupying space nor inherence in an occupant of space is a negative attribute. This negative attribute is distinct from that particular essence. When this distinctness is established, it is established that it is not impossible for that particular essence to be known while being heedless of that negative attribute, unlike the case if his particular essence were occupying space, because assuming that were the case, its occupying space would be that very essence itself, not something distinct from it; so it would be impossible for him to know that particular essence while being heedless of occupying space. And since it is not so, it is established that that particular essence cannot be occupying space; thus the difference between the two cases becomes clear. As for the third question, its answer: If it were established that animals perceive their particular selves while being oblivious to the reality of occupying space, then the question would be necessary; however, there is no way to know this premise. Then, supposing we affirm for them abstract souls, yet they need not be rational souls, because abstraction from matter is a negative attribute, and sharing in negative attributes does not imply equality in the complete quiddity.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents three objections and their answers concerning the argument that the soul is an abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad). The first objection is not explicitly stated here but likely claimed that the proof applies to all objects, not just humans. The author begins by answering the first objection: he revisits the proof from the chapter on the negation of hyle (prime matter) that a body occupying space cannot be an attribute in another; it is a self-subsisting entity. He then applies this: the human self either occupies space or not. If it did, then knowing the self would require knowing it as an occupant of space, but one can be aware of one's self while entirely unaware of being an occupant of space (e.g., when intensely focused). Thus the self cannot be a body, so it must be an abstract substance. The second objection argues that the same reasoning could show that the abstract substance itself is not known because one might be unaware of its negative attribute (not being a body). The author distinguishes: the negative attribute (not being an occupant) is distinct from the essence. So knowing the essence without the attribute is possible, unlike the case where the essence is identical to the attribute (as with a body where occupying space is the essence itself). The third objection concerns animals: if the proof works for humans, it should also work for animals, implying they have rational souls. The author replies that we do not know if animals have self-awareness of this kind; even if they do, they could have abstract souls without rationality, because abstraction is a negative attribute shared by many, but rationality is a positive quiddity not implied by mere abstraction.

Key Terms
القيد السلبي
الجوهر المجرد
المتحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if the soul is abstract, then to know it one must be aware of its abstraction (non-bodiliness), otherwise it cannot be known.
Correction: The author shows that abstraction is a negative attribute distinct from the essence; one can know the essence without being aware of the negative attribute.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the second objection is not necessary?
A) Because the abstract substance is not knowable at all.
B) Because the negative attribute is distinct from the essence, so knowledge of the essence does not require knowledge of the attribute.
C) Because the objector's argument is based on a false premise.
D) Because the soul is not actually abstract.

Key Takeaway
Negative attributes like 'not a body' are distinct from the essence, so knowing the essence does not require being aware of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your soul's essence can be known without focusing on what it is not. When you look inward, do you define yourself by your physical body, roles, and possessions—or do you seek the core of your being? What would it take to turn your attention away from these external frames and directly experience your own soul?
?
The objector in this lesson thought that ignorance of the soul's non-bodiliness would make the soul unknown. Are there negative attributes in your life—things you are not—that you hold onto as part of your identity? Could letting go of them open a more direct knowledge of yourself?
?
The lesson shows that for a body, its essence is identical to its being a body. If you reduce yourself to your material existence, do you risk losing the true essence that God has placed within you? Commit today to spend five minutes in silent introspection, focusing on your inner reality rather than your body or achievements.

Do Animals Have Rational Souls? Third Objection Answered
Answer to third objection: animals and rational souls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument for the human soul being abstract does not necessarily apply to animals, and why abstraction alone does not imply rationality.

Core Idea
Abstraction from matter does not guarantee rationality.

The third objection extends the argument to animals: if self-awareness proves the soul is abstract, then animals with self-awareness would have rational souls. The author replies that we cannot verify whether animals have such self-awareness, and even if they do, abstraction from matter is a negative attribute shared by many beings, whereas rationality is a positive quiddity not implied by mere abstraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the third objection: the proof used for humans would also apply to animals, so animals would have rational abstract souls. Then read the author's two-part answer.
The answer has two parts: one about the premise, the other about the conclusion.
2
First part: uncertainty about animal self-awareness
The author says we have no way to know if animals perceive their particular selves while oblivious to being bodies. Why is this important?
The proof for humans relied on that specific experience; we cannot assume it for animals.
3
Second part: abstraction does not imply rationality
Even if animals have abstract souls, they need not be rational. Abstraction from matter is a negative attribute (qayd salbi), and sharing negative attributes does not mean sharing the entire quiddity. Rationality is a positive property.
This shows the difference between essential and accidental attributes.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this answer preserves the uniqueness of the human rational soul while allowing for other abstract beings (like angels? But animals are not rational).

?

?

Sixth Proof: The Soul is Not a Body – Three Premises and the Unity of Perception
Al-Razi presents a sixth proof for the soul's non-bodily nature with three premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's sixth proof that the soul is not a body, including its three premises and the detailed argument for the first premise based on the unity of perception.

Core Idea
The soul must be a single, non-bodily entity because it unifies perceptions from all senses and faculties.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a sophisticated argument for the immateriality of the soul. He begins by outlining three premises that together lead to the conclusion that the soul is neither a body nor a bodily entity. The first premise, which he elaborates in detail, shows that the same entity must perceive colors, sounds, images, and abstract meanings. This establishes the unity of the perceiving subject as a non-physical reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three premises
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then locate where al-Razi states the three premises. Notice the structure: the conclusion is stated first, then the premises. The first premise is elaborated with examples.
The three premises are enumerated explicitly in the text.
2
Examine the proof of the first premise through sensory example
Focus on the sensory example about honey and voice recognition. Ask yourself: why must the perceiver be the same entity for color and taste? What does this show about the soul?
The principle 'the judge must have both items present' is key.
3
Extend to imagination, estimation, and thinking
Trace how the argument extends from sensory perception to imagination, estimation, and thinking. In each case, the same entity is needed to compare and judge. Underline the repeated pattern: 'the judge over two things must have them present'.
The pattern shows the unity of the soul across different faculties.
4
Understand the conclusion that the soul is one non-bodily entity
Synthesize the argument: the same entity perceives, imagines, estimates, and thinks. This entity cannot be a body because bodily parts would be separate. Write down the conclusion in your own words.
The conclusion is stated at the beginning: the soul is not a body nor a bodily entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة في المسألة: أن نقول: لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود شيء واحد يكون موصوفاً بجميع أنواع الإدراكات لجميع أنواع المدركات ويكون هو المباشر لتلك الأفعال. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، وجب أن تكون النفس غير جسم، وغير جسماني. وهذا الدليل، إنما يتم بتقرير مقدمات ثلاثة: أولها: إن الموصوف بجميع الإدراكات شيء واحد. وثانيها: إن ذلك الشيء، كما أنه هو الموصوف بجميع الإدراكات فكذلك هو الفاعل لجميع الأفعال. وثالثها: إنه لما كان الأمر كذلك، وجب أن يقال: النفس ليست جسماً، ولا حالة في الجسم. أما المقدمة الأولى: فالدليل على صحتها: إنا إذا رأينا لون العسل حكمنا بأن طعمه: الحلاوة. والقاضي على الشيئين، لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فوجب وجود شيء يكون هو بعينه مدركاً للألوان والطعوم، حتى يمكنه أن يحكم بأن هذا الملون هو ذلك المطعوم. وأيضاً: إذا سمعنا صوتاً مخصوصاً حكمنا بأن صاحب هذا الصوت هو فلان، الذي صورته كذا وكذا، فلا بد ههنا من شيء واحد، يكون هو بعينه سامعاً للأصوات، ومبصراً للصور، حتى يمكنه أن يحكم بأن صاحب هذا الصوت هو بعينه صاحب تلك الصورة. فثبت: أنه لا بد من وجود شيء يكون هو بعينه مدركاً لجميع المحسوسات بالحواس الخمسة. ثم نقول: إن ذلك الحاس هو بعينه المتخيل. وذلك لأنا إذا تخيلنا صورة زيد، أو تخيلنا صوته، أو تخيلنا طعم العسل أو رائحته، أو حرارة النار، أو برودة الماء. فإذا أحسسنا بإحدى الحواس الخمسة أحد هذه المحسوسات حكمنا: بأن هذا المحسوس هو بعينه ذلك المتخيل، والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فوجب الإقرار بوجود شيء يكون بعينه الحساس ويكون هو بعينه ذلك المتخيل. ثم نقول: إن القوى الوهمية عند القوم: عبارة عن قوة تحكم على الأشخاص المحسوسة بمعان جزئية غير محسوسة، مثل: الحكم على هذا الشخص بأنه عدو، وعلى ذلك الآخر بأنه صديق، والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فوجب الاعتراف بوجود شيء واحد يكون هو بعينه مدركاً لهذه الصور، ولهذه المعاني، حتى يمكنه أن يحكم على تلك الصور بهذه المعاني. ثم نقول: القوة المفكرة هي التي تقوى أولاً على تركيب الصور بعضها بالبعض، وثانياً على تركيب المعاني بعضها بالبعض، وثالثاً على تركيب بعض الصور ببعض المعاني، ورابعاً على تحليل بعضها عن البعض. ولا شك أن هذا التركيب والتحليل حكم على البعض بثبوت البعض الآخر، أو بسلبه عنه، والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فلا بد من شيء واحد يكون هو الحساس للكل، ويكون هو بعينه المتخيل للكل، ويكون هو بعينه المدرك للصور والمعاني، ويكون هو بعينه المتصرف فيها بأسرها بالتركيب تارة، وبالتحليل أخرى. ثم نقول: إنه يمكننا أن نحكم على هذا الشخص بأنه إنسان وأنه ليس بفرس. والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما، فلا بد من شيء واحد يكون هو بعينه مدركاً لهذا الجزئي ولهذا
The sixth proof concerning the question: that we say: It is necessary to acknowledge the existence of one thing that is described by all types of perceptions of all types of perceptibles, and that it is the direct agent of those actions. And when the matter is so, it is necessary that the soul (nafs) be not a body (jism), nor a bodily entity (jismānī). This proof is only completed by establishing three premises: First: that the one described by all perceptions is one thing. Second: that that thing, just as it is the one described by all perceptions, so also it is the doer of all actions. Third: that when the matter is so, it must be said: the soul is not a body, nor a state in a body. As for the first premise: the proof of its validity is: we, when we see the color of honey, judge that its taste is sweetness. And the judge over two things must have the two judged things present, so it is necessary that there exist a thing that is itself the perceiver of colors and tastes, so that it can judge that this colored thing is that tasted thing. Also: when we hear a particular voice, we judge that the owner of this voice is so-and-so, whose image is such-and-such. So here there must be one thing that is itself the hearer of sounds and the seer of images, so that it can judge that the owner of this voice is exactly the owner of that image. So it is established: that there must exist a thing that is itself the perceiver of all sensed things (maḥsūsāt) through the five senses. Then we say: that that senser (ḥāss) is exactly the imaginer (mutakhayyil). That is because when we imagine the image of Zayd, or imagine his voice, or imagine the taste of honey or its smell, or the heat of fire, or the cold of water, then if we sense through one of the five senses one of these sensed things, we judge: that this sensed thing is exactly that imagined thing, and the judge over two things must have the two judged things present, so it is necessary to affirm the existence of a thing that is itself the senser and that is itself that imaginer. Then we say: the estimative faculties (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) according to them is a faculty that judges about sensed individuals by particular non-sensed meanings, such as judging that this person is an enemy, and that other is a friend, and the judge over two things must have the two judged things present, so it is necessary to admit the existence of one thing that is itself the perceiver of these images and these meanings, so that it can judge upon those images by these meanings. Then we say: the thinking faculty (al-quwwa al-mufakkira) is that which is able firstly to combine images with each other, secondly to combine meanings with each other, thirdly to combine some images with some meanings, and fourthly to separate some from others. And there is no doubt that this combination and separation is a judgment of some things by the affirmation of others, or by their negation, and the judge over two things must have the two judged things present, so there must be one thing that is the senser of all, and that is itself the imaginer of all, and that is itself the perceiver of images and meanings, and that is itself the disposer of them all by combination sometimes and by separation other times. Then we say: it is possible for us to judge of this person that he is a human and that he is not a horse. And the judge over two things must have the two judged things present, so there must be one thing that is itself the perceiver of this particular and of this
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the soul (nafs) is not a body (jism) nor a bodily entity. The argument is structured around three premises. The first premise asserts that the subject of all perceptions is one single thing. The second premise adds that this same thing is also the agent of all actions. The third premise concludes that such a thing must be non-bodily. The author then elaborates the first premise with several examples: when we see the color of honey and judge it to be sweet, the same entity must perceive both color and taste to compare them; when we hear a voice and recognize the person, the same entity must hear the sound and see the image; and similarly for imagination, estimation, and thinking. In each case, the judge (al-qāḍī) comparing two items must have them both present, implying a single perceiving subject. This leads to the conclusion that the soul is a single, immaterial entity that performs all these functions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that different senses or brain parts separately perceive different stimuli, and that the soul is a composite or physical.
Correction: The argument shows that a single entity must unify perceptions from all senses and faculties (imagination, estimation, thinking). This entity cannot be a body because bodily parts would be separate and unable to compare and judge across modalities.

Checkpoint
What principle does al-Razi repeatedly use to argue for a single perceiver?
A) The perceiver must be divisible.
B) The judge over two things must have the two judged things present.
C) The soul is a subtle body.
D) Perceptions are caused by the brain.

Key Takeaway
The soul is a single immaterial substance that perceives, imagines, judges, and composes meanings, and thus cannot be a body or bodily entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are constantly judging between what you see, hear, imagine, and think. Have you ever realised that it is the same 'you' doing all of this? If your soul is one and non-bodily, why do you often live as if you are scattered among your desires and distractions? What would it take for you to gather yourself back into that unity?
?
When you sin, do you consider that your soul—the very same entity that perceives truth and beauty—is the one committing the act? If the soul is immaterial and accountable, how does this change the weight you place on your inner motives versus your outward actions? Will you start examining your intentions today?
?
Al-Razi argues that the soul must be one because it compares and judges across senses. Think of a time you felt torn between what your body wanted and what your conscience told you. In that moment, which part of you was truly 'you'? If the soul is the real you, are you feeding it with what it needs, or are you starving it while catering to your body?

The Origin of Psychic Faculties: Spirit, Brain, and Refutation
Ibn Sina quotes Peripatetics on the spirit receiving psychic faculties without brain or liver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the psychic faculties cannot pre-exist in the spirit without the brain and how this refutation establishes the brain's role in the soul's faculties.

Core Idea
Psychic faculties are not pre-loaded in the spirit; they arise only when the spirit meets the brain.

In this passage, Ibn Sina cites a Peripatetic claim that psychic faculties flow directly from the spirit without needing bodily organs like the brain or liver. He then refutes this view, arguing that fairness (al-insaf) does not permit it. The author of al-Matalib al-Aliyah comments that this refutation implies the faculties are not pre-existent in the spirit but emerge after the spirit reaches the brain and is shaped by its temperament. This sets the stage for understanding the soul-body relationship in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two main parts: the paraphrased Peripatetic view and the author's refutation with commentary.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لكن الإنصاف لم يسوغ هذا المذهب وأبطله' – it signals the refutation.
2
Understand the Peripatetic view
The Peripatetics claimed that all psychic faculties (al-quwa al-nafsaniyya) emanate directly from the spirits (al-arwah) without needing the brain or liver as preparatory organs. Ask: What does this imply about the role of bodily organs?
3
Grasp the refutation and its implication
Ibn Sina refutes this view. The author explains that the refutation shows the faculties were not present in the spirit before reaching the brain. They arise only after the spirit is 'tempered' by the brain's temperament. What does 'tahduth' (تحدث) tell us about their origin?
Contrast this with the idea that faculties are pre-existent.
4
Connect to the larger theme
This passage establishes the brain as a necessary condition for psychic faculties. Consider how this fits into the broader debate about the soul-body relationship in Islamic philosophy.
5
Reflect on the method
Note the use of 'al-insaf' (fairness) as a criterion for rejecting a philosophical view. How does this reflect the method of rational inquiry?
This is a common tool in philosophical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في أول كتاب : الأدوية القلبية : وقوم من أصحاب الحكيم الأجل ، قالوا في القوى النفسانية : إنها كلها تفيض من الأرواح من غير حاجة للروح إلى الأعضاء الأخرى، كالدماغ والكبد في الاستعداد لقبولها لكن الإنصاف لم يسوغ هذا المذهب وأبطله . وأقول : هذا تصريح بأن الروح قبل انتقاله إلى الدماغ لم تكن القوى النفسانية موجودة . وإنما تحدث فيه بعد انتقاله إلى الدماغ وتكيفه بكيفيته
He said in the beginning of the book 'The Cardiac Medicines': A group of the followers of the most noble sage said concerning the psychic faculties: that they all flow from the spirits without the spirit needing the other organs, like the brain and the liver, for preparedness to receive them. But fairness did not permit this doctrine and he refuted it. And I say: This is an explicit statement that before the spirit's transfer to the brain, the psychic faculties were not existent. Rather, they arise in it after its transfer to the brain and its being tempered by its temperament.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents a citation from Ibn Sina's 'Adwiya al-Qalbiya' (Cardiac Medicines) followed by the author's own commentary. It begins by reporting the view of some Peripatetic philosophers who claimed that all psychic faculties (al-quwa al-nafsaniyya) emanate directly from the spirits (al-arwah) without requiring the brain or liver as preparatory organs. The author notes that fairness did not permit this doctrine and Ibn Sina refuted it. In the commentary (Aqulu: 'I say'), the author clarifies that this refutation implies the psychic faculties are not present in the spirit before it reaches the brain; rather, they arise (tahduth) in the spirit after it enters the brain and is tempered by the brain's temperament (kayfiyya). Thus, the brain is essential for the actualization of these faculties.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the faculties are not existent before the spirit's transfer to the brain; they arise only after transfer and tempering by the brain's temperament.

Key Takeaway
The psychic faculties are not pre-existing in the spirit but arise when the spirit reaches the brain and is modified by its temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What belief about yourself might you need to re-examine in light of this lesson?

Ibn Sina's Argument on the Spirit's Specialization and Unity of the Soul
Ibn Sina explains how the spirit loses certain faculties when it reaches the brain or liver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina uses the specialization of the spirit in different organs to argue for the unity of the soul.

Core Idea
The spirit loses potentials to gain focus, not add new powers.

This passage from Ibn Sina's Kitab al-Hayawan presents a vivid physiological analogy. The spirit, initially capable of multiple functions, becomes specialized when it reaches the brain or liver. Ibn Sina uses this to show that the soul is one entity that manifests through different faculties, and any change in power is due to removal of obstacles, not addition. Understanding this helps clarify the Islamic philosophical view of the soul's unity and its relation to the body.

Guided Path
1
Follow the spirit's journey
Trace the spirit from its initial state to its specialization at brain and liver. Notice how it loses certain potentials.
The spirit is initially fit for multiple actions like nutrition and growth.
2
Distinguish essential vs accidental change
Identify the two types of change: the spirit becomes weak essentially, or stronger accidentally due to obstacle removal.
Accidental strengthening occurs because an obstacle is removed, not because something is added.
3
Connect to the soul's unity
See how Ibn Sina concludes that the soul is one, using this argument as evidence.
The argument shows that the same spirit can become specialized, implying a single underlying soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الفصل السابع من المقالة الثانية عشرة من كتاب الحيوان من الشفاء : إن الروح الذي يأتي الدماغ ، فإنه يصلح في جوهره الأول أيضاً لأعمال أخرى مثل التغذية والتنمية ، وغير ذلك . فاذا اعتدل بطل استعداده لتلك القوى ، وصار غير مغذٍ وانفرد بفعل واحد ، ولم تترادف عليه الأفعال ، فيشتغل بعضها عن بعض . وكذلك إذا صار إلى الكبد ، أبطل مزاج الكبد عند الاستعداد لفعل الحس والحركة ، وتركه خاصاً لفعل التغذية . فهذه الأعضاء التي تغذي القلب ، إنما تغير المزاج، فتصير الروح عديمة القوة . وهذا بالذات ، وتصير الروح أقوى قوة من القوة الباقية . وهذا بالعرض . لأن تلك القوة إنما ازدادت قوتها ، لأن القوة التي زالت كانت كالعائق لهذه القوة الباقية عن الفعل فلما زال العائق ، كملت القوة ، لا لأنه حدث أمر زائد ، بل لأنه زال العائق . ثم قال : وعلى هذه الحجة يصح أن يطرد القول بأن النفس واحدة
And he said in the seventh chapter of the twelfth article of the Book of Animals from al-Shifa': that the spirit that comes to the brain – it is fit in its first substance also for other works like nutrition and growth and other things. So when it is balanced, its preparedness for those faculties becomes invalid, and it becomes non-nutritive and becomes isolated for one action, and actions are not successive upon it, so that some would distract from others. Likewise, when it goes to the liver, the temperament of the liver cancels its preparedness for the act of sensation and movement, and leaves it specific for the act of nutrition. These organs that nourish the heart – they only change the temperament, so the spirit becomes devoid of power. And this is essential, and the spirit becomes stronger in power than the remaining power. And this is accidental. Because that power only increased its power because the power that disappeared was like an obstacle to this remaining power from acting. So when the obstacle was removed, the power was perfected, not because an additional thing occurred, but because the obstacle was removed. Then he said: And upon this argument it is valid to extend the statement that the soul is one.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a citation from Ibn Sina's 'Kitab al-Hayawan' (Book of Animals) from al-Shifa', followed by a brief commentary. It describes the fate of the spirit when it reaches the brain or liver. Initially, the spirit in its first substance (jawharuhu al-awwal) is fit for multiple functions like nutrition, growth, etc. However, when it reaches the brain and becomes balanced (i'tadala), it loses its preparedness for those other faculties and becomes specialized for a single action, such as perception. Similarly, when it reaches the liver, its temperament cancels the preparedness for sensation and movement, restricting it to nutrition. The author then explains that the organs nourishing the heart alter the spirit's temperament, making it either weak (adima al-quwwa) essentially, or stronger accidentally due to the removal of an obstacle. This is a key argument for the unity of the soul: the spirit, when focused, becomes one power. The quote ends with Ibn Sina stating that this argument supports the proposition that the soul is one.

النفس واحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The spirit gains new powers at each organ.
Correction: The spirit loses potentials to gain focus, and any strengthening is due to removal of obstacles, not addition.

Checkpoint

A) It gains the power of nutrition.
B) It loses all powers.
C) It becomes specialized for one action.
D) It combines all powers.

Key Takeaway
The spirit's specialization in different organs demonstrates that the soul is one and its powers are not added but revealed through removal of hindrances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Sina says the spirit becomes 'non-nutritive' when it reaches the brain. The spirit’s power increases accidentally because an obstacle is removed, not because something new is added. Think of one deep-rooted habit or attachment that is blocking your spiritual growth. Will you remove it, or do you secretly prefer to keep it because it feels comfortable? What will you do about it this week?
?
If the soul is one, then your entire spiritual struggle is not about acquiring new virtues but about clearing away what hinders your innate God-given potential. What is the single greatest hindrance in your heart right now? Confront it, and decide right now to begin removing it.

The Heart as the First Origin of Faculties
Ibn Sina's statement on the heart as source of faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's claim that the heart is the first origin from which all faculties flow to the brain and liver.

Core Idea
Faculties originate from the heart before reaching the brain and liver.

In this passage from al-Shifa', Ibn Sina presents a physiological model where the heart is the primary source of all faculties. From it, faculties flow to the brain for higher functions like imagination and thinking, and to the liver for nutrition. This lesson examines his statement.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's statement
Read the Arabic quotation carefully. Notice how Ibn Sina describes the heart as 'mabda' awwal' (first origin).
This is a key point: the heart is the source, not the brain or liver.
2
Trace the flow of faculties
Identify the two pathways: (1) heart to brain (imagination and thinking) then to rest of body; (2) heart to liver (nutrition) then to rest of body.
Some faculties complete their actions in the brain.
3
Reflect on the hierarchy
Consider why Ibn Sina might place the heart as the primary origin. The commentary in the next lesson will clarify the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الفصل الثامن من المقالة الخامسة من علم النفس من طبيعيات الشفاء : القلب مبدأ أول وتفيض منه إلى الدماغ قوى . فبعضها تتم أفعالها في الدماغ، وأجزائه كالتخيل والتفكر ، ثم تفيض منه إلى سائر الأعضاء وأيضاً يفيض من القلب إلى الكبد قوة التغذية ، ثم منها إلى سائر الأعضاء . أقول : هذا تصريح بأن القوى الطبيعية والنفسانية موجودة في الخارج قبل انتقال الروح إلى الدماغ والكبد ، وأن الحاجة إلى حصول الروح فيه وفي الكبد لظهور أفعال القوى عنها ، لا لحدوثها في أنفسها
And he said in the eighth chapter of the fifth article of the Psychology from the Natural Sciences of al-Shifa': The heart is a first origin, and from it faculties flow to the brain. Some of them complete their actions in the brain and its parts, like imagination and thinking, then they flow from it to the rest of the organs. Also, from the heart flows to the liver the power of nutrition, then from it to the rest of the organs. I say: This is an explicit statement that the natural and psychic faculties exist externally before the spirit's transfer to the brain and liver, and that the need for the spirit to be present in them (the brain and liver) is for the manifestation of the faculties' actions, not for their occurrence in themselves.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a citation from Ibn Sina's 'Psychology' (al-Shifa') followed by the author's commentary. Ibn Sina describes the heart as the first origin (mabda' awwal) from which faculties (quwa) flow to the brain. In the brain, some faculties like imagination (takhayyul) and thinking (tafakkur) complete their actions, then these faculties flow from the brain to the rest of the body. Similarly, the power of nutrition (quwwat al-taghdhiya) flows from the heart to the liver, then from the liver to other organs. The author's commentary clarifies that this passage explicitly states that the natural faculties (al-quwa al-tabi'iyya) and psychic faculties (al-quwa al-nafsaniyya) exist externally (i.e., in actuality) before the spirit's transfer to the brain and liver. The role of the brain and liver is not to create these faculties, but to provide a locus for their actions (zuhur af'al al-quwa) to manifest. This counters the idea that these faculties arise only upon the spirit's arrival at the organs.

?
If the heart is truly the first origin, then purifying it is not optional—it is foundational. What specific impurities in your heart (like pride, envy, or heedlessness) have you been neglecting, and what concrete step will you take today to cleanse this origin so that your faculties may flow rightly?
