Faculties Pre-exist Before Their Manifestation in Organs
Author's commentary on pre-existence of faculties and role of organs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's clarification that natural and psychic faculties exist externally before the spirit's transfer to the brain and liver, and that these organs only manifest the faculties' actions.

Core Idea
Faculties exist in reality before they manifest in specific organs.

After citing Ibn Sina, the author adds an important interpretive point: The brain and liver do not generate the faculties; rather, they are loci for the manifestation of pre-existing faculties. This counters a potential misunderstanding that faculties arise only when the spirit reaches these organs.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's commentary
Read the Arabic commentary and its translation. Pay attention to the phrase 'موجودة في الخارج قبل انتقال الروح' (exist externally before the spirit's transfer).
This is the key claim.
2
Identify the two points
The author makes two main points: (1) faculties exist externally before the spirit reaches the brain and liver; (2) the need for the spirit in those organs is for the manifestation of the faculties' actions, not for their origination.
These points directly counter a common misunderstanding.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this affects the understanding of the heart as first origin. The heart seems to be the locus for the initial emanation of faculties, but they pre-exist in a different sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الفصل الثامن من المقالة الخامسة من علم النفس من طبيعيات الشفاء : القلب مبدأ أول وتفيض منه إلى الدماغ قوى . فبعضها تتم أفعالها في الدماغ، وأجزائه كالتخيل والتفكر ، ثم تفيض منه إلى سائر الأعضاء وأيضاً يفيض من القلب إلى الكبد قوة التغذية ، ثم منها إلى سائر الأعضاء . أقول : هذا تصريح بأن القوى الطبيعية والنفسانية موجودة في الخارج قبل انتقال الروح إلى الدماغ والكبد ، وأن الحاجة إلى حصول الروح فيه وفي الكبد لظهور أفعال القوى عنها ، لا لحدوثها في أنفسها
And he said in the eighth chapter of the fifth article of the Psychology from the Natural Sciences of al-Shifa': The heart is a first origin, and from it faculties flow to the brain. Some of them complete their actions in the brain and its parts, like imagination and thinking, then they flow from it to the rest of the organs. Also, from the heart flows to the liver the power of nutrition, then from it to the rest of the organs. I say: This is an explicit statement that the natural and psychic faculties exist externally before the spirit's transfer to the brain and liver, and that the need for the spirit to be present in them (the brain and liver) is for the manifestation of the faculties' actions, not for their occurrence in themselves.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a citation from Ibn Sina's 'Psychology' (al-Shifa') followed by the author's commentary. Ibn Sina describes the heart as the first origin (mabda' awwal) from which faculties (quwa) flow to the brain. In the brain, some faculties like imagination (takhayyul) and thinking (tafakkur) complete their actions, then these faculties flow from the brain to the rest of the body. Similarly, the power of nutrition (quwwat al-taghdhiya) flows from the heart to the liver, then from the liver to other organs. The author's commentary clarifies that this passage explicitly states that the natural faculties (al-quwa al-tabi'iyya) and psychic faculties (al-quwa al-nafsaniyya) exist externally (i.e., in actuality) before the spirit's transfer to the brain and liver. The role of the brain and liver is not to create these faculties, but to provide a locus for their actions (zuhur af'al al-quwa) to manifest. This counters the idea that these faculties arise only upon the spirit's arrival at the organs.

Key Terms
قوى طبيعية
قوى نفسانية
روح
ظهور أفعال القوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The brain and liver generate the faculties.
Correction: The author clarifies that the brain and liver only manifest pre-existing faculties; they do not cause the faculties to occur.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the natural and psychic faculties?
A) They come into existence when the spirit reaches the brain and liver.
B) They exist externally before the spirit's transfer to the brain and liver.
C) They are created by the brain and liver.
D) They are illusions.

Key Takeaway
Faculties exist in reality before they become active in the brain or liver; these organs only provide the conditions for their actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You possess faculties like reason and will that exist before you act on them. When have you failed to manifest a good quality because you waited for external conditions, even though the capacity was already within you? What will you do today to act on a latent faculty?
?
The author teaches that the spirit activates what already exists, not what is created anew. If your spiritual potential is already given by Allah, what is stopping you from allowing His guidance to manifest in your actions? Are you prepared to remove the barriers?
?
If you truly believed that your deepest faculties are pre-existing gifts from Allah, how would that change your approach to developing character? Write down one specific faculty (like patience or gratitude) and commit to one action to manifest it today.

Ibn Sina's View on the Heart's Role in Al-Qanun
Ibn Sina's Qanun states the heart gives all faculties; three possibilities are extracted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's claim that the heart gives all faculties to the other organs, and appreciate that from his words three possibilities are derived, even though they are not fully enumerated here.

Core Idea
The heart is the source of all bodily faculties according to Ibn Sina's al-Qanun.

In this passage, the author cites a key text from Ibn Sina's medical masterpiece, al-Qanun, where the primacy of the heart is affirmed. The heart is said to bestow all four categories of faculties—nourishing, growing, perceiving, moving—upon the rest of the body. This sets the stage for extracting a limited set of possible interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's statement
Read the Arabic carefully to see how Ibn Sina attributes all four types of faculties to the heart as the source.
2
Note the starting phrase 'وهذا الوجه'
This phrase indicates that Ibn Sina's view was already mentioned earlier and now the author is giving a specific quotation.
3
Understand the term 'الروح الحامل للقوى النفسانية'
This incomplete sentence mentions a 'spirit carrying psychic faculties', which is key to the three possibilities, but the passage cuts off.
The three possibilities are not listed here, but they are said to be derived from the words.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
Notice that the lesson ends mid-sentence. The three possibilities will presumably be explained on the next page. This lesson is a setup.
This is a good moment to consider what those possibilities might be.

وهذا الوجه هو الذي اختاره في فصل الأعضاء من كتاب القانون : فإنه قال فيه : القلب يعطي سائر الأعضاء كلها : القوى التي تغذي والتي تنمي والتي تدرك والتي تحرك . واعلم : أنه قد تلخص من هذه الكلمات احتمالات ثلاثة لا مزيد عليها . وذلك لأن الكل اتفقوا على أن الروح الحامل للقوى النفسانية ،

And know: that from these words three possibilities are summarized with no addition. That is because all agreed that the spirit carrying the psychic faculties,
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a conclusion from the previous quotations. It first cites Ibn Sina's 'al-Qanun' (The Canon of Medicine) where he states that the heart gives all other organs all faculties: nourishing, growing, perceiving, and moving. Then the author begins to summarize that from all these words three possibilities (ihtimalat) can be derived, with no more. However, the passage is cut off before the three possibilities are enumerated. The incomplete sentence mentions that all agree that the spirit carrying the psychic faculties... The lesson is therefore incomplete and serves mainly to introduce the summary.

First Premise: Receptivity is Essential
Author presents the first premise and its proof via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the proof for the creation of souls: that the soul's receptivity to accidental attributes is a necessary property (من اللوازم) of its essence, and how this is proven using an infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
The soul's capacity to change is essential, not accidental—otherwise an infinite chain would follow.

The author begins by observing that souls clearly undergo change—they gain knowledge and character traits after lacking them. This establishes that souls are receptive to accidents. He then argues that this receptivity must be an essential necessity (لازم) of the soul, not a separable accident. If it were separable, an infinite regress would result, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening observation
Read the first few lines where the author states that souls become knowledgeable and acquire traits after not having them. This is the empirical basis for the proof.
The author takes this as obvious—no one denies that souls change.
2
Identify the key claim and the infinite regress argument
Notice that the author asserts the receptivity must be essential (من اللوازم). Then follow the proof: if it were accidental, each receptivity would require another, leading to an infinite chain of causes that occur simultaneously—an impossibility.
The infinite regress (تسلسل) is a standard refutation tool in kalam.
3
Reflect on why infinite regress is impossible here
The author says the regress would be in causes and effects that exist simultaneously. Think about why that is absurd: it would require an endless series of simultaneous dependencies, which cannot be actual.
This is different from a temporal regress; it's a logical regress in simultaneous causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول : أن نقول : لا شك أن هذه النفوس قابلة للصفات الحادثة ، فإنها تصير عالمة بأشياء ، بعد ما أنها ما كانت عالمة بها ، وتصير موصوفة [ بأخلاق ، بعد أنها ما كانت موصوفة (١) ] بها . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن قابلية الصفات الحادثة ، يجب أن يكون من لوازم ذواتها . وقابلية الصفات الحادثة حادثة ، فيلزم أن يقال : إنها لا تخلو عن الحوادث ، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، فهذه النفوس حادثة . فيفتقر في تقرير هذه المقدمات [ إلى مقدمتين (۱) ] . المقدمة الأولى : في أن قابلية هذه الحوادث من لوازم ذواتها . والدليل عليه : أن تلك القابلية إن لم تكن من اللوازم ، كانت من العوارض المفارقة ، فتكون تلك الذوات قابلة لتلك القابلية . فقابلية تلك القابلية ، إن كانت من اللوازم فهو المقصود ، وإن كانت من العوارض ، افتقرت كل قابلية إلى تقدم قابلية أخرى . ولزم التسلسل في أسباب ومسببات توجد دفعة واحدة . وهو محال . فثبت : أن قابلية هذه الصفات من لوازم [ ذوات (۲) ] هذه النفوس . والمقدمة الثانية : في بيان أن قابلية الصفات الحادثة : حادثة . والدليل عليه : أن كون تلك الحوادث مقبولة للغير، صفة عارضة لذواتها . وإمكان الصفة موقوف على إمكان الموصوف . ولما كان حصول الحادث في الأزل محالاً كان كون الصفة الحادثة مقبولاً للغير، أولى بأن يمتنع كونه أزلياً . وهذا يدل على أن قابلية الصفات الحادثة : حادثة . وعند تقرير هاتين المقدمتين، ظهر أن هذه النفوس لا تنفك عن الحوادث ، [ فنقول : وكل (۳) ] ما لا ينفك عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، بالوجوه المذكورة في مسألة حدوث الأجسام وقد ذكرناها هناك فظهر بهذا الدليل : أن كل ما كان قابلاً للحوادث ، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون قديماً . ، لكن الأجسام والنفوس البشرية قابلة للحوادث ، فيمتنع كونها قديمة [ ثم نقول (4) : ] والإله تعالى قديم فيمتنع كونه قابلا للحوادث . فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل
The first aspect: that we say: There is no doubt that these souls are receptive to accidental attributes (صفات حادثة), for they become knowledgeable of things after not having been knowledgeable of them, and they become described with [character traits, after not having been described] with them. When this is established, we say: Receptivity to accidental attributes must be among the necessities (لوازم) of their essences. And receptivity to accidental attributes is accidental (حادثة), so it follows that they are not free from accidents (حوادث), and everything that is not free from accidents is originated (حادث), so these souls are originated. The establishment of these premises requires [two premises]. The first premise: that the receptivity of these accidents is among the necessities of their essences. The proof of it: that if that receptivity is not among the necessities, it would be among the separable accidents (عوارض مفارقة), so those essences would be receptive to that receptivity. Then the receptivity of that receptivity, if it is among the necessities, it is the intended, and if it is among the accidents, each receptivity would require a preceding receptivity, and an infinite regress (تسلسل) would follow in causes and effects that occur simultaneously, which is impossible. So it is established: that the receptivity of these attributes is among the necessities [of the essences] of these souls. The second premise: the demonstration that receptivity to accidental attributes is accidental. The proof of it: that the fact that those accidents are receptive to something else is an accidental attribute of their essences. And the possibility of the attribute depends on the possibility of the subject (الموصوف). And since the occurrence of an accident in pre-eternity (الأزل) is impossible, the fact that an accidental attribute is receptive to something else is more deserving of being impossible to be eternal. And this indicates that receptivity to accidental attributes is accidental. And when these two premises are established, it appears that these souls are not free from accidents, [and we say: and everything] that is not free from accidents is originated, by the ways mentioned in the issue of the creation of bodies, and we have mentioned them there. So it appears by this proof that everything that is receptive to accidents is impossible to be eternal (قديم). But bodies and human souls are receptive to accidents, so it is impossible for them to be eternal. [Then we say:] And God the Exalted is eternal, so it is impossible for Him to be receptive to accidents. This is the completion of the speech on establishing this proof.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage contains a full proof (الوجه الأول) for the createdness of the human soul. It is an argument structured with two premises. The author first asserts that souls acquire knowledge and character traits after not having them, proving they are receptive to accidental attributes. He then argues that this receptivity must be essential (من لوازم الذوات) and itself accidental. The first premise is proved by an infinite regress argument: if receptivity were not essential, it would be accidental, requiring further receptivity ad infinitum. The second premise is proved by noting that receptivity to accidents is itself an accident, and since accidents cannot be eternal, it must be originated. From these premises, it follows that souls cannot be free from accidents, and everything not free from accidents is originated. The author then applies the same reasoning used for bodies to souls, concluding that souls are created. Finally, he distinguishes God, who is eternal and not receptive to accidents.

Key Terms
قابلة (receptive)
صفات حادثة (accidental attributes)
لوازم الذات (essential necessities)
عوارض مفارقة (separable accidents)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the soul's ability to change is just a temporary accident that comes and goes.
Correction: The author proves that the very capacity to receive change must be a permanent essential property, otherwise an infinite regress of capacities would result.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the soul's receptivity to accidents cannot be a separable accident?
A) Because the soul never actually changes.
B) Because if it were separable, the soul would need another receptivity to receive it, leading to an infinite regress.
C) Because revelation says so.
D) Because the soul is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The soul's receptivity to accidental change is an essential property, proven by the impossibility of an infinite regress of simultaneous causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul's capacity to change is essential, not accidental, what does that mean for your struggle with a bad habit you've considered 'just part of who you are'? Will you now treat it as changeable, or continue to excuse it?
?
You have just learned that an infinite regress is impossible. Look at your own life: are you putting off learning or growth, assuming you will 'change later'? What is keeping you from taking that step now?
?
The soul's receptivity to truth is essential. When you last ignored a truth that made you uncomfortable, did you realize you were suppressing something built into your very nature? How will you respond differently next time?

Second Premise and Conclusion: Souls are Created
Author presents the second premise and completes the proof that souls are created, then distinguishes God from created beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise of the proof—that receptivity to accidents is itself accidental (حادثة)—and how this leads to the conclusion that souls are created, while God is not receptive and therefore eternal.

Core Idea
Receptivity to accidents cannot be eternal; therefore souls are created, and God is not receptive.

Having proven that the soul's receptivity to change is an essential property, the author now addresses whether that receptivity itself is eternal or created. He argues that since accidents cannot occur in pre-eternity, the capacity to receive them must also be originated. This completes the proof that souls are not free from accidents and are therefore created. Finally, he contrasts souls with God, who is eternal and cannot be receptive to accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise and its proof
Read the passage beginning with 'المقدمة الثانية'. Notice how the author argues that the fact of being receptive to accidents is itself an attribute of the soul, and since accidents cannot be eternal, this attribute cannot be eternal either.
The key phrase: 'وإمكان الصفة موقوف على إمكان الموصوف' — the possibility of the attribute depends on the possibility of the subject.
2
Understand the conclusion: souls are created
After establishing both premises, the author concludes that souls are not free from accidents, and everything not free from accidents is originated. This is the same reasoning used for bodies.
The author refers to discussions in the 'issue of the creation of bodies'.
3
See the distinction from God
The final lines draw a contrast: God is eternal, so He cannot be receptive to accidents. This reinforces the doctrine of God's immutability.
This also serves as a corollary: only created things are receptive to accidents.

?

?
God is not receptive to accidents—meaning He is never changed by what happens in your life. How does that truth challenge the way you complain about your circumstances?
?

5 min
The Temporality of Souls from Receptivity to Accidents
Establishes two premises proving souls are temporal due to their receptivity to accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's receptivity to accidents entails its temporality, and why this proof prevents the soul from being eternal.

Core Idea
The soul cannot be eternal because it necessarily receives temporal accidents.

The author builds a rigorous proof for the temporality of souls using two premises. First, he shows that the soul's receptivity to accidents is an essential property, not a separable one. Second, he demonstrates that this receptivity itself must be temporal. From these, it follows that souls are continuously subject to accidents and therefore must be temporal, just like bodies. This lesson unpacks each step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
Notice the two distinct premises and the final conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
The text states: 'The first premise: that the receptivity to these accidents is among the necessities of their essences.' and 'The second premise: an explanation that the receptivity to temporal attributes is temporal.' Find these in the passage.
3
Analyze the proof for premise 1
The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: if receptivity were a separable accident, an infinite regress would follow. Understand why this is impossible.
The argument relies on the impossibility of simultaneous infinite regress of causes.
4
Analyze the proof for premise 2
The proof argues that a temporal attribute cannot be preeternal. Since accidents are temporal, the receptivity to them must also be temporal.
This mirrors the argument for the temporality of bodies.
5
Apply the conclusion
The author concludes that souls are never free of accidents, so they are temporal. Then he distinguishes God, who is eternal and not receptive to accidents.
This establishes a clear difference between created souls and the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحوادث فهو حادث ، فهذه النفوس حادثة . فيفتقر في تقرير هذه المقدمات إلى مقدمتين . المقدمة الأولى : في أن قابلية هذه الحوادث من لوازم ذواتها . والدليل عليه : أن تلك القابلية إن لم تكن من اللوازم ، كانت من العوارض المفارقة ، فتكون تلك الذوات قابلة لتلك القابلية . فقابلية تلك القابلية ، إن كانت من اللوازم فهو المقصود ، وإن كانت من العوارض ، افتقرت كل قابلية إلى تقدم قابلية أخرى . ولزم التسلسل في أسباب ومسببات توجد دفعة واحدة . وهو محال . فثبت : أن قابلية هذه الصفات من لوازم ذوات هذه النفوس . والمقدمة الثانية : في بيان أن قابلية الصفات الحادثة : حادثة . والدليل عليه : أن كون تلك الحوادث مقبولة للغير، صفة عارضة لذواتها . وإمكان الصفة موقوف على إمكان الموصوف . ولما كان حصول الحادث في الأزل محالاً كان كون الصفة الحادثة مقبولاً للغير، أولى بأن يمتنع كونه أزلياً . وهذا يدل على أن قابلية الصفات الحادثة : حادثة . وعند تقرير هاتين المقدمتين، ظهر أن هذه النفوس لا تنفك عن الحوادث ، فنقول : وكل ما لا ينفك عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، بالوجوه المذكورة في مسألة حدوث الأجسام وقد ذكرناها هناك . فظهر بهذا الدليل : أن كل ما كان قابلاً للحوادث ، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون قديماً . لكن الأجسام والنفوس البشرية قابلة للحوادث ، فيمتنع كونها قديمة . ثم نقول : والإله تعالى قديم فيمتنع كونه قابلا للحوادث . فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل.
Accidents are temporal, so these souls are temporal. It requires for establishing these premises two premises. The first premise: that the receptivity to these accidents is among the necessities of their essences. The proof of it: if that receptivity is not among the necessities, then it would be among separable accidents, so those essences would be receptive to that receptivity. Then the receptivity of that receptivity, if it is among the necessities, that is the intended point, and if it is among the accidents, every receptivity would require a preceding receptivity, and an infinite regress would follow in causes and effects that exist simultaneously, which is impossible. So it is established that the receptivity to these attributes is among the necessities of the essences of these souls. The second premise: an explanation that the receptivity to temporal attributes is temporal. The proof of it: that those accidents being receptive to another is an accidental attribute to their essences. The possibility of the attribute depends on the possibility of the attribute-bearer. Since the occurrence of a temporal thing in pre-eternity is impossible, the attribute being temporal and receptive to another is more fitting that its being pre-eternal is impossible. This indicates that the receptivity to temporal attributes is temporal. When these two premises are established, it becomes clear that these souls are not free of accidents. So we say: everything that is not free of accidents is temporal, by the methods mentioned in the issue of the temporality of bodies, and we have mentioned them there. So it appears by this proof that everything that is receptive to accidents, it is impossible for it to be eternal. But bodies and human souls are receptive to accidents, so it is impossible for them to be eternal. Then we say: God the Exalted is eternal, so it is impossible for Him to be receptive to accidents. This completes the discussion on establishing this proof.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the temporality of souls (nufūs) based on their receptivity to accidents (ḥawādith). It is a structured argument with two premises and a conclusion, following the method used for bodies. The argument first establishes that the soul's receptivity to accidents is a necessary attribute of its essence, otherwise an infinite regress would occur. Second, it proves that this receptivity itself must be temporal, because a temporal attribute cannot be pre-eternal. From these it follows that souls are never free of accidents, and whatever is never free of accidents is temporal—just like bodies. Consequently, souls cannot be eternal. The passage concludes by affirming that God, being eternal, cannot be receptive to accidents.

Key Terms
النفوس (souls)
الحوادث (accidents/temporal events)
القابلية (receptivity)
اللوازم (necessities)
التسلسل (infinite regress)
القديم (eternal/pre-eternal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is entirely simple and thus cannot be receptive to accidents.
Correction: The author argues that receptivity to accidents is a necessary attribute (lāzim) of the soul's essence, not a separable accident. Thus, being receptive does not compromise the soul's simplicity.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first premise about the soul's receptivity to accidents?
A) It is a separable accident.
B) It is a necessary attribute of the soul's essence.
C) It is impossible.
D) It is pre-eternal.

Key Takeaway
Souls are temporal because they necessarily and temporally receive accidents, unlike God who is eternal and receptive of nothing.

?
If the soul were eternal, it would be receptive to accidents in pre-eternity, which is impossible. Your existence had a beginning. What weight does this give to your responsibility toward your Creator? Are you living as if you will never die and be held to account?

Two Eternals Lead to Infinite Regress
Sanusi argues that two distinct eternals imply infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two distinct eternal beings forces each to be composed of an infinite number of eternal parts, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Two distinct eternals require infinite composition, an absurdity.

Sanusi presents a logical argument against polytheism. He assumes that if a second eternal existed alongside God, they would share the attribute of pre-eternity but differ in some other respect. This difference forces each eternal to be composite, leading to an infinite regress of parts. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the assumption
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi starts by stating the assumption: if another eternal existed, there would be two eternals. Then he defines pre-eternity as negation of prior non-existence.
Pre-eternity is an attribute, not nothing.
2
Understand the need for distinction
The two eternals share pre-eternity. To be two distinct entities, they must differ in some other respect. Otherwise they would be identical.
This sets up the dilemma.
3
Trace the infinite regress
If they differ, each eternal is composed of two parts: one for shared pre-eternity and one for distinction. Each part is itself eternal, so the same reasoning applies, leading to an infinite number of parts. This is impossible.
The argument shows that any difference forces composition, and composition repeats ad infinitum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : أن نقول : لا شك أن الله تعالى قديم ، فلو حصل قديم آخر ، لزم القول بوجود قديمين . وذلك محال . والدليل عليه : أن القدم عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق ، فيكون صفة موجودة. والقديمان لما اشتركا في القدم، فقد اشتركا في أمر موجود . فإما أن يختلفا باعتبار آخر ، وإما أن لا يكونا كذلك ، فإن اختلفا باعتبار آخر كان القدم الذي به المشاركة مغاير لذلك الاعتبار ، الذي به المخالفة . فيكون ذلك القديم مركباً من جزءين ، وكل واحد من جزئي القديم : قديم ، فذانك الجزءان بعد اشتراكهما في القدم، لا بد وأن يختلفا باعتبار آخر ، وإلا لم يكن أحدهما بكونه جهة الاشتراك ، والآخر بكونه جهة الامتياز أولى من العكس . والكلام فيه كما في الأول فيلزم كون ذلك القديم مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية . وذلك محال . ولما ثبت هذا ثبت أن القديم الثاني ، يجب أن لا يخالف الأول ، في جزء من أجزاء الماهية . فوجب أن يتماثلا مطلقاً ، فيلزم كون الأرواح البشرية مماثلة للإله تعالى في جميع الصفات . وظاهر أنه محال . فهذا بيان أنه يمتنع وجود موجودين قديمين.
The second aspect: that we say: there is no doubt that God the Exalted is eternal. If another eternal existed, it would necessitate the statement of the existence of two eternal beings. That is impossible. The proof of it: pre-eternity is an expression of the negation of prior non-existence, so it is an existing attribute. The two eternals, since they share in pre-eternity, they share in an existing matter. Either they differ in another respect, or they are not so. If they differ in another respect, then the pre-eternity by which there is sharing is different from that respect by which there is difference. Then that eternal would be composed of two parts, and each of the two parts of the eternal is eternal. Those two parts, after their sharing in pre-eternity, must differ in another respect, otherwise one of them being the aspect of sharing and the other being the aspect of distinction would not be more fitting than the reverse. The discussion regarding it is as in the first, so it necessitates that eternal being composed of infinite parts. That is impossible. And when this is established, it is established that the second eternal must not differ from the first in any part of the quiddity. So they must be absolutely similar, thus necessitating that human souls are similar to God the Exalted in all attributes. That is clearly impossible. So this is an explanation that it is impossible for two eternal existents to exist.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the existence of two eternal beings (qadīmayn). It argues that if a second eternal existed alongside God, they would share the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) but must differ in some respect to be distinct. This difference would require each eternal to be composed of two parts: one for shared pre-eternity and one for distinctness. Since each part is itself eternal, the same reasoning applies, leading to an infinite regress of parts, which is impossible. Alternatively, if they do not differ, they would be identical, making human souls equal to God—an absurdity. Therefore, only one eternal being can exist.

Key Terms
قديم (eternal)
القدم (pre-eternity)
الاشتراك (sharing)
الامتياز (distinction)
التركيب (composition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that composition here refers to physical parts, but it is logical composition of attributes.
Correction: The argument deals with conceptual parts of the essence (quiddity), not physical components.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what happens if two eternals differ in a respect other than pre-eternity?
A) They become identical.
B) Each eternal becomes composed of two eternal parts, leading to infinite regress.
C) The later eternal is temporal.
D) Pre-eternity is not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
The existence of two distinct eternals forces each to be composed of an infinite number of eternal parts, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only God is truly eternal, what does that mean for the things you cling to that you treat as unchangeable or permanent? Is there any relationship, possession, or status you treat as though it will never end? How does this truth dismantle that illusion?
?
The argument shows that two eternals lead to absurdity. God's uniqueness is non-negotiable. Are there any 'co-lords' in your life—people, desires, or fears—that you functionally treat as equal to God in authority? What step will you take today to dethrone them?
?
This proof is abstract but it guards the very foundation of faith. If you truly internalize that God alone is eternal and all else is contingent, how should that change your daily priorities? Write one concrete commitment you can make today that reflects this truth.

The Absurdity of Complete Similarity
Sanusi shows that without distinction, two eternals would be identical, a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if two eternals do not differ in any respect, they would be completely similar, which leads to the absurd conclusion that human souls equal God.

Core Idea
No difference means identical eternals, making humans like God.

Having shown that two distinct eternals are impossible, Sanusi now considers the alternative: that the second eternal does not differ from the first in any part of its essence. This leads to absolute similarity, implying that human souls would be divine-like attributes, an obvious absurdity. Let's see this final step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional
Read the passage and note that it begins with 'when this is established', referring to the impossibility of two distinct eternals. The only remaining possibility is that the second eternal does not differ at all from the first.
This is the other horn of the dilemma.
2
Identify the conclusion of absolute similarity
If no difference exists in any part of the quiddity (essence), then the two eternals are completely similar (متماثلان). In kalam, this means they are identical in all attributes.
Similarity here implies essential identity.
3
Recognize the absurd consequence
If human souls are eternal (as some philosophers claim), then they would be completely similar to God, meaning they would have all divine attributes. This is clearly impossible, as it would imply polytheism or equality with God.
The absurdity confirms that a second eternal cannot exist.

If they differ in another respect, then the pre-eternity by which there is sharing is different from that respect by which there is difference. The First Objection for the Eternity of Souls
Presents the first argument from infinite celestial cycles for the eternity of souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first philosophical argument for the eternity of souls, which uses the premise of infinite celestial cycles to argue that an infinite number of souls must exist now.

Core Idea
If bodies are infinite, but souls are temporal, an infinite number of souls must now exist—an impossibility.

Proponents of the eternity of souls argued that souls cannot originate temporally. Their first argument relies on the philosophical claim that celestial cycles are infinite, leading to an infinite number of human bodies. If souls come into existence with their bodies, the same infinity would apply to souls, resulting in an actual infinite number of souls—something they argue is impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step, setting the stage for its later refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Pay attention to the words 'غير متناهية' and 'عدد'.
2
Identify the logical flow
Break the argument into its components: premise (infinite cycles), conditional (if souls temporal then condition), conclusion (infinite souls now), and impossibility (number finite).
The conclusion is presented as impossible, so the argument leads to a contradiction.
3
Reflect on the key claim about numbers
Note the claim that every number admits increase and decrease, hence is finite. This is a crucial premise in the argument's reductio.
This is a point of potential refutation: can an actual infinite exist?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بقدم النفوس بوجهين : الحجة الأولى : إنه ثبت في الفلسفة : أن الأدوار الفلكية غير متناهية فالأبدان البشرية غير متناهية . فلو كانت النفوس البشرية حادثة ، لكان حدوثها ، لأجل أن حدوث البدن شرط في فيضانها عن العلل العالية . فلما كانت الأبدان غير متناهية ، وجب أن تكون النفوس البشرية غير متناهية . لكنه ثبت أن النفوس لا تقبل الفساد ، فوجب أن يوجد الآن عدد لا نهاية له من النفوس . وذلك محال لأن كل عدد فإنه يحتمل الزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو متناه
And those who claim the eternity of souls argued by two aspects: The first argument: it is established in philosophy that the celestial cycles are infinite, so human bodies are infinite. If human souls were temporal, their temporal origination would be because the occurrence of the body is a condition for their emanation from the higher causes. Since the bodies are infinite, human souls must be infinite. But it is established that souls do not accept corruption, so it must be that there now exists an infinite number of souls. That is impossible because every number admits increase and decrease, and everything that is so is finite.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from proponents of the eternity of souls (qidam al-nufūs). They argue that since celestial cycles are infinite (as per philosophy), human bodies are infinite in number. If souls originated with their bodies, there would be an infinite number of souls now, which is impossible because any number can be increased or decreased—so it must be finite. Therefore, souls cannot be temporal; they must be eternal. The refutation of this argument is not included in this window, likely on a later page.

Key Terms
قدم النفوس
الأدوار الفلكية
غير متناهية
الفساد
عدد
متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument proves the necessity of an actual infinite existing, concluding that souls must be eternal.
Correction: The argument does not prove the eternity of souls; it only shows that if the premises are accepted, temporal origination leads to an impossible conclusion. The author intends to refute these premises (e.g., infinite cycles or emanation theory) elsewhere.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first argument for the eternity of souls?
A) Souls are infinite in number now.
B) Souls are temporal.
C) Celestial cycles are finite.
D) Bodies are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for the eternity of souls tries to show that temporal origination leads to an actual infinite number of souls, which is deemed impossible because any number is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This argument presents a logical contradiction if souls are temporal. Has reading it shaken your confidence in the temporal nature of your own soul? Where do you turn to resolve that tension?

Proof of the Impossibility of Two Eternal Beings
Author proves impossibility of two eternal beings via composition and regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the coexistence of two eternal beings is logically impossible according to the argument of infinite regress of composition.

Core Idea
Two eternal beings cannot coexist because they would be composite and lead to infinite regress.

This passage presents a rigorous proof that Allah alone is eternal. The author argues that if there were two eternal beings, they would share the attribute of pre-eternity, leading to composition and an infinite chain of distinctions. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument uses a conditional: if two eternal beings, then impossible.
2
Understand the definition of pre-eternity (qidam)
The author defines pre-eternity as the negation of prior non-existence. It is an existing attribute. This definition is crucial because it means that when two beings share pre-eternity, they share something real.
3
Follow the two-case analysis
The author considers two possibilities: either the two eternal beings differ in some other respect, or they do not. Trace how each case leads to a contradiction.
The first case leads to composition and infinite regress; the second leads to absolute similarity.
4
See how difference leads to composition and infinite regress
If they differ, then each eternal being has two aspects: the shared pre-eternity and the distinguishing feature. This makes each eternal being composite. Each part is eternal, and the argument repeats, leading to an infinite number of parts, which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the assumption of two eternal beings.
5
Conclude the impossibility and the implication of absolute similarity
If they do not differ, they are absolutely similar in all attributes, which would make human souls equal to God, an obvious impossibility. Therefore, the initial assumption is false: there cannot be two eternal beings.
This completes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخر، لزم القول بوجود قديمين. وذلك محال.
والدليل عليه: أن القدم عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق، فيكون صفة موجودة. والقديمان لما اشتركا في القدم، فقد اشتركا في أمر موجود. فإما أن يختلفا باعتبار آخر، وإما أن لا يكونا كذلك، فإن اختلفا باعتبار آخر كان القدم الذي به المشاركة مغاير لذلك الاعتبار الذي به المخالفة. فيكون ذلك القديم مركباً من جزءين، وكل واحد من جزئي القديم: قديم، فذانك الجزءان بعد اشتراكهما في القدم، لا بد وأن يختلفا باعتبار آخر، وإلا لم يكن أحدهما بكونه جهة الاشتراك، والآخر بكونه جهة الامتياز أولى من العكس. والكلام فيه كما في الأول فيلزم كون ذلك القديم مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية. وذلك محال.
ولما ثبت هذا ثبت أن القديم الثاني، يجب أن لا يخالف الأول، في جزء من أجزاء الماهية. فوجب أن يتماثلا مطلقاً، فيلزم كون الأرواح البشرية مماثلة للإله تعالى في جميع الصفات. وظاهر أنه محال. فهذا بيان أنه يمتنع وجود موجودين قديمين.
Another, it would necessitate saying there are two eternal beings. That is impossible. The proof for it is: that pre-eternity (qidam) is an expression of the negation of prior non-existence, so it is an existing attribute. Since the two eternal beings share in pre-eternity, they share in something existent. Either they differ in another respect, or they do not. If they differ in another respect, then the pre-eternity by which they share is different from that respect by which they differ. Then that eternal being would be composed of two parts, each part of the eternal being is eternal. Then those two parts, after they share in pre-eternity, must necessarily differ in another respect; otherwise one of them being the aspect of sharing and the other being the aspect of distinction would not be more appropriate than the reverse. The discussion regarding them is as in the first, so it would necessitate that eternal being being composed of infinite parts. That is impossible. And when it is established that this is established, the second eternal being must not differ from the first in any part of the essence (mahiyya). So they must be absolutely similar, which would necessitate human souls being similar to God Most High in all attributes. It is clear that this is impossible. This is an explanation that the existence of two eternal beings is impossible.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a proof demonstrating the impossibility of two eternal beings. The author argues that if there were two eternal beings, they would share the attribute of pre-eternity (al-qidam), which is an existing attribute. Since they share this, they must have something in common. To be distinct, they must differ in some other respect. If they differ, then each eternal being would have two aspects: a shared part (pre-eternity) and a distinguishing part. Thus they would be composite. Each part would also be eternal. Then those two parts would again share pre-eternity and need a further differentiating aspect, leading to an infinite regress of parts. Therefore, an eternal being cannot be composite. Since this regress is impossible, it cannot be that there are two eternal beings. Moreover, if there were two distinct eternal beings, they would have to differ in at least one respect; but the regress argument shows that any such difference leads to composition. Hence they would have to be identical in all respects, which would make human souls equal to God, which is absurd.

Key Terms
قديم (eternal/preexistent)
قدم (pre-eternity)
صفة موجودة (existing attribute)
ماهية (essence)
جزء (part)
تركيب (composition)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two eternal beings could coexist if they are distinct in essence but share the attribute of pre-eternity.
Correction: The argument shows that sharing pre-eternity forces either composition (and infinite regress) or absolute similarity, both impossible, so they cannot coexist.

Checkpoint
The author argues that if two eternal beings existed, they would necessarily:
A) Be composed of parts
B) Be absolutely similar
C) Both of the above
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The existence of two eternal beings leads to logical contradictions (infinite regress or absolute similarity, both impossible), proving that only one eternal being, Allah, exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even for a moment, considered the possibility of another eternal being besides Allah, such as the universe itself? How does this logical proof challenge that thought, and are you willing to submit your intellect to this reasoning even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
This proof shows that any shared attribute between two eternal beings leads to composition and impossibility. In your daily life, do you treat anything—like your wealth, status, or relationships—as if it were co-eternal with Allah, something that exists independently? Will you uproot that from your heart today?
?
If you truly internalize that only Allah is eternal and everything else is contingent, how will your anxieties about the future change? Does knowing that nothing else deserves ultimate trust make you willing to place your absolute reliance on Him alone, right now?

Second Argument for the Pre-Eternity of Souls and Its Refutation
The author presents and then refutes the argument that souls' future eternality necessitates pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical steps of the argument that future eternality necessitates pre-eternity and the refutation that a temporal effect can arise from an eternal cause under a condition.

Core Idea
Future eternality does not force past pre-eternality when causation is conditional.

This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical argument. Proponents of the pre-eternity of souls argue that since souls are immortal (eternal in the future), they cannot have been originated—otherwise they would be receptive to non-existence. The author then refutes this by carefully analyzing the nature of causality, showing that an eternal cause can produce a temporal effect when its action depends on a temporal condition. Let's trace the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Start by reading the full Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a proof by contradiction, an objection, and a final refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the contradiction
The proponent claims that if souls were originated, they would have an inherent receptivity to non-existence. But since they are eternal in the future, this receptivity cannot be actualized. Therefore, they must be pre-eternal.
This is a classic kalam argument from屬性 (sifat).
3
Examine the causal analysis
The author presents an objection: souls might become necessary through a cause. The response analyzes different scenarios: pre-eternal unconditional cause leads to pre-eternity; temporal or conditional cause leads to possible cessation or infinite regress.
Focus on the logic of tasalsul (infinite regress).
4
Grasp the refutation's key insight
The refutation introduces the Active Intellect as the cause, which is pre-eternal but its effect depends on the origination of the body. Thus, the soul can be originated yet remain eternal in the future.
This breaks the link between future eternality and pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: قالوا: النفوس الناطقة أبدية. فتكون أزلية أما الأول فهو ثابت بالاتفاق، وبالدليل أيضاً الذي سيجيء ذكره.
وأما الثاني: فالدليل عليه: أنها لو كانت حادثة لكانت ماهيتها قابلة للعدم. وتلك القابلية من لوازم الذات، فوجب أن تكون قابلة للعدم أبداً وإذا كان كذلك، كانت قابلة للعدم بعد الوجود. وقد فرضنا أنها غير قابلة للعدم بعد الوجود هذا خلف. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: هذه النفوس قابلة للعدم، نظراً إلى ذواتها وحقائقها، إلا أنها تصير واجبة الوجود لوجوب عللها؟
قلنا: إن كانت عللها قديمة، وما كان تأثيرها فيها موقوفاً على شرط حادث أو كان موقوفاً على شرط قديم، فحينئذ يلزم قدمها. وإن كانت عللها حادثة، أو إن كانت قديمة إلا أن تأثيرها في وجودها يكون موقوفاً على شرط حادث، فتلك العلة الحادثة، وذلك الشرط الحادث: يكون جائز الزوال لذاته. فإن كان واجب البقاء نظراً إلى علته، عاد التقسيم الأول فيه، ولزم التسلسل. وهو محال. وإن كان جائز الزوال فحينئذ لزم من جواز زواله، جواز زوال النفوس بعد وجودها. فثبت بهذا البيان: أن النفوس لو لم تكن ازلية، لم تكن أبدية، لكنها أبدية، فوجب كونها أزلية.
وأجيب عنه: بأن علة وجود النفوس البشرية هي العقل الفعال. إلا أن فيضان هذا المعلول عن تلك العلة القديمة مشروط بحدوث البدن، المستعد.
The second proof: They said: The rational souls (nufus natiqa) are eternal (abadiyya). So they are pre-eternal (azaliyya). As for the first, it is established by agreement and by the proof which will come later. As for the second, the proof for it is: If they were originated (haditha), their quiddity (mahiyya) would be receptive to non-existence. That receptivity is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence, so they must be receptive to non-existence eternally. If so, they would be receptive to non-existence after existence. But we have assumed they are not receptive to non-existence after existence—this is a contradiction (khalaf). If they say: Why is it not permissible to say: These souls are receptive to non-existence with respect to their essences and realities, but they become necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) due to the necessity of their causes? We say: If their causes are pre-eternal and their effect is not dependent on a temporal condition, then pre-eternity would be necessary. Or if their causes are originated, or if they are pre-eternal but their effect depends on a temporal condition, then that temporal cause and condition are possibly transient in themselves. If it is necessary of existence in view of its cause, the first division returns and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is impossible. If it is possibly transient, then the possibility of its cessation entails the possibility of the souls ceasing after existence. So it is established by this explanation that if souls were not pre-eternal, they would not be eternal; but they are eternal, so they must be pre-eternal. And it was answered: The cause of existence of human souls is the Active Intellect (al-‘aql al-fa‘‘al), but the emanation of this effect from that pre-eternal cause is conditioned by the origination of the prepared body...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the pre-eternity of souls and its refutation. The proponent argues: Souls are eternal (abadiyya, everlasting in the future). If they were originated, they would have the potential for non-existence, but since they are immortal, that potential cannot be actualized. Therefore, they must be pre-eternal (azaliyya). The author then presents a potential objection: perhaps souls are receptive to non-existence by essence but become necessary through their cause. The author then critically examines this: If the cause is pre-eternal and unconditional, the souls would be pre-eternal, contradicting the claim they are originated. If the cause is originated or conditional, then the condition is transient, so the souls could cease. Hence the only way to maintain both origination and eternal future is impossible. Therefore, the souls must be pre-eternal if they are eternal. Then the author gives a response: the cause is the Active Intellect, a pre-eternal cause, but its effect is conditional upon a temporal condition (the body's origination), so the effect is originated even though the cause is pre-eternal. This avoids the problem.

النفوس الناطقة (rational souls)
أبدية (eternal/everlasting)
أزلية (pre-eternal)
قابلية (receptivity)
ذات (essence)
علة (cause)
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
جائز الزوال (possibly transient)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
العقل الفعال (Active Intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument conflates pre-eternity (azaliyya) with eternal future (abadiyya). Some think that if something is eternal in the future, it must have always existed.
Correction: The refutation shows that a temporal effect can arise from an eternal cause if the cause's action is conditioned on a temporal event. Thus, future eternality does not entail past pre-eternality.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why can souls be originated yet eternal in the future?
A) Because their cause is pre-eternal but conditioned
B) Because they are simple
C) Because they are not receptive to non-existence
D) Because they are necessary by essence

Key Takeaway
Souls can be originated and yet eternal in the future because their eternal cause (Active Intellect) only produces them when a temporal condition (the body) is present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is destined for eternity yet originated in time, what condition—like the 'prepared body'—are you setting now to ensure that your soul's eternal future is one of bliss rather than loss? What specific action will you take today to prepare that condition?
?
The refutation shows that an eternal cause can act conditionally. Where in your life do you passively assume that God's will overrides your responsibility, ignoring the conditions He has placed? Will you stop making excuses and attend to them?
?
This argument was not just an academic exercise—it concerns the very nature of your soul. Do you truly live as one whose soul will never cease, or do your daily choices betray a forgetfulness of that eternal reality? How will you remind yourself tomorrow morning?

The Traditionalist View and Two Solutions on Resurrection
Sanusi presents traditionalist belief and two solutions from mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditionalist belief that man is only the body and the two methods proposed by mutakallimūn to reconcile it with resurrection.

Core Idea
Resurrection of the same person requires reconciling body and identity.

This lesson presents the problem that arises when one accepts both that man is merely the physical body and that there will be a resurrection. The author first describes the traditionalist stance, then introduces two attempted solutions from the mutakallimūn: the re-creation of the non-existent exactly as it was, and the persistence of parts with loss of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Note the flow from traditionalist belief to the two solutions.
The author uses a contrastive structure: 'As for... they believed... then...'
2
Identify the traditionalist belief and the two methods
Find where the author states the traditionalist view on human reality, and then locate the two methods (first and second) proposed by the mutakallimun.
The first method allows re-creation of the non-existent; the second asserts parts persist but attributes perish.
3
Reflect on the author's initial assessment
Notice that the author calls the second method 'extremely far-fetched'. Ask yourself why he might say that before reading the critique.
This sets up the critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أهل التقليد والأثر . فهم قد اعتقدوا أنه لا حقيقة للإنسان إلا هذا الهيكل . وسمعوا من الأنبياء والعلماء : إثبات القول بالبعث والقيامة ، فاعتقدوا كلا القولين ولم تصل خواطرهم إلى أن الجمع بين هذين القولين ، هل هو ممكن لا ؟ ثم إن جماعة من المتكلمين لما أرادوا أن يجمعوا بين هذين القولين ذكروا فيه طريقين : الأول : قال بعضهم : إن إعادة المعدوم بعينه جائز . والثاني : قال آخرون : إنه محال . إلا أنه إذا مات الإنسان ، فإنه لا تفنى تلك الأجزاء أو تلك الذوات ، وإنما يفنى المزاج والصفات . فإذا أعيد التركيب والمزاج إلى تلك الأجزاء ، كان الإنسان العائد، عين الإنسان الذي كان موجودا في الدنيا . وهذا في غاية البعد ، لأنا نقول : الإنسان المعين إما أن يكون عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء كيف كانت ، أو عينها بشرط كونها موصوفة بصفات مخصوصة . والأول باطل . لأن تلك الأجزاء حال ما كانت خالية عن العلم والحياة والقدرة والمزاج والتأليف ، كانت موجودة ، مع أن هذا الإنسان [ ما كان موجوداً . فإذا مات هذا الإنسان وزالت هذه الصفات . فإن تلك الأجزاء تكون باقية مع أن ذلك الإنسان قد لا يكون باقياً . وهذا يدل على أن هذا الإنسان ] ليس عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء [ كيف كانت ، بل إن كان ولا بد ، فالإنسان المعين عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء ] المخصوصة بشرط قيام صفات مخصوصة بها فإذا عدمت تلك الصفات ، فقد عدم أحد الأجزاء المقمومة لماهية ذلك الإنسان المعين ، وحينئذ يحصل الجزم بأن ذلك الإنسان قد عدم وفني . ثم عند هذا نقول : إن كانت إعادة المعدوم ممكنة ، فلا حاجة إلى القول ببقاء تلك الذوات ، وإن كانت غير ممكنة لم يكن القول ببقاء تلك الذوات كافياً في القول بصحة عود ذلك الإنسان بعينه
As for the people of tradition and transmission, they believed that there is no reality for man except this physical structure. They heard from the prophets and scholars the affirmation of the resurrection and the standing, so they believed both statements, but their thoughts did not reach whether combining these two statements is possible or not. Then a group of mutakallimūn, when they wanted to combine these two statements, mentioned two methods regarding it: First: Some said that the re-creation of the non-existent exactly as it was is permissible. Second: Others said that it is impossible, except that when a man dies, those parts or those essences do not perish; rather, the temperament and attributes perish. So if composition and temperament are restored to those parts, then the returning man is the very same man who existed in the world. This is extremely far-fetched, for we say: The specific man is either an expression of those parts however they are, or the very same with the condition that they are described with specific attributes. The first is false because those parts, when they were devoid of knowledge, life, power, temperament, and composition, were existing, while this man [was not existing. So when this man dies and these attributes cease, those parts remain while that man may not remain. This indicates that this man] is not an expression of those parts [however they are; rather, if it must be, the specific man is an expression of those parts] specific with the condition that specific attributes are established in them. So when those attributes cease, one of the components of the essence of that specific man has ceased, and then certainty is obtained that that man has ceased and perished. Then we say: If the re-creation of the non-existent is possible, then there is no need for the affirmation of the survival of those essences; and if it is not possible, then the affirmation of the survival of those essences is not sufficient for affirming the soundness of the return of that very same man.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the common belief that man is merely the physical body. The author explains that traditionalists (ahl al-taqlīd wa al-athar) accepted both that man is only this body and that there will be a resurrection, without considering whether these two can be reconciled. Mutakallimūn proposed two solutions: first, that God can create the non-existent exactly as it was (iʿādat al-maʿdūm bi ʿaynihi); second, that the parts (ajzāʾ) or essences (dhawāt) never perish, only the temperament and attributes do, so restoring composition to the same parts brings back the same man. The author rejects both as far-fetched. He argues that the specific human (al-insān al-muʿayyan) must be defined either by the parts irrespective of attributes, or by the parts conditioned by specific attributes. The first is false because the parts can exist without the attributes (like life, knowledge) that characterize the living man; thus the parts alone do not constitute the man. So the second definition is forced: the specific man is the parts with specific attributes. When those attributes vanish, one component of the essence is lost, and the man is destroyed. Then the author concludes: if re-creation of the non-existent is possible, the survival of parts is unnecessary; if it is impossible, survival of parts is insufficient for the return of the same man. The passage thus shows the theological dilemma of personal identity in resurrection.

Key Terms
اهل التقليد والأثر
إعادة المعدوم
الأجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Man is only the physical body and resurrection is straightforward.
Correction: The author shows that these two beliefs are not easily reconciled, requiring complex solutions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the traditionalists (ahl al-taqlīd wa al-athar) believe about the reality of man?
A) Man has both a body and a soul.
B) There is no reality for man except this physical structure.
C) Man is a composite of parts and attributes.
D) Man is essentially his soul.

Key Takeaway
The traditionalist view and the mutakallimūn's solutions fail to adequately reconcile bodily identity with resurrection, leading to the author's critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever hold beliefs about God or the afterlife that you have never examined for compatibility? Are you willing to sit with the tension this lesson highlights and seek deeper understanding, even if it challenges your assumptions?
?
If your identity is not simply your physical body, then who are you really? How does this lesson push you to consider that your ultimate existence depends entirely on God's will, not on any material continuity? Will you trust Him to re-create you as the same 'you' even if everything about you changes?
?
The traditionalists accepted resurrection without questioning how it could be possible. In your own life, have you ever avoided difficult questions about your faith because they made you uncomfortable? What would it take for you to confront those questions with humility and seek the truth as the mutakallimun did?

Critique of Defining Man by Parts Alone
Sanusi critiques definitions of the specific human and concludes on resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the definition of man as parts without attributes and the implications for resurrection.

Core Idea
The specific human is parts with specific attributes, not just any parts.

In this continuation, the author critiques the second solution by analyzing what constitutes a specific human being. He argues that if man is defined by parts irrespective of attributes, the definition fails because parts can exist without the living attributes. Instead, man must be defined by parts with specific attributes, so when attributes are lost, the person ceases to exist. This leads to a dilemma regarding the possibility of re-creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique carefully
Read the Arabic and translation from 'for we say' to the end. Pay attention to the logical steps.
This is a dense argument; read slowly.
2
Understand the two definitions and the refutation of the first
Identify the two definitions of 'specific human' (al-insān al-muʿayyan). Note why the first definition (parts how ever they are) is rejected: parts exist without attributes that make a person alive.
The author uses an example: parts devoid of knowledge, life, etc., exist but the human does not.
3
Grasp the conclusion and the final dilemma
After establishing that the specific human requires parts with attributes, note what happens when attributes vanish: the man perishes. Then follow the two conditional statements about re-creation of the non-existent.
The dilemma shows that either survival of parts is unnecessary or insufficient.

وأما أهل التقليد والأثر . فهم قد اعتقدوا أنه لا حقيقة للإنسان إلا هذا الهيكل . وسمعوا من الأنبياء والعلماء : إثبات القول بالبعث والقيامة ، فاعتقدوا كلا القولين ولم تصل خواطرهم إلى أن الجمع بين هذين القولين ، هل هو ممكن لا ؟ ثم إن جماعة من المتكلمين لما أرادوا أن يجمعوا بين هذين القولين ذكروا فيه طريقين : الأول : قال بعضهم : إن إعادة المعدوم بعينه جائز . والثاني : قال آخرون : إنه محال . إلا أنه إذا مات الإنسان ، فإنه لا تفنى تلك الأجزاء أو تلك الذوات ، وإنما يفنى المزاج والصفات . فإذا أعيد التركيب والمزاج إلى تلك الأجزاء ، كان الإنسان العائد، عين الإنسان الذي كان موجودا في الدنيا . وهذا في غاية البعد ، لأنا نقول : الإنسان المعين إما أن يكون عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء كيف كانت ، أو عينها بشرط كونها موصوفة بصفات مخصوصة . والأول باطل . لأن تلك الأجزاء حال ما كانت خالية عن العلم والحياة والقدرة والمزاج والتأليف ، كانت موجودة ، مع أن هذا الإنسان [ ما كان موجوداً . فإذا مات هذا الإنسان وزالت هذه الصفات . فإن تلك الأجزاء تكون باقية مع أن ذلك الإنسان قد لا يكون باقياً . وهذا يدل على أن هذا الإنسان ] ليس عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء [ كيف كانت ، بل إن كان ولا بد ، فالإنسان المعين عبارة عن تلك الأجزاء ] المخصوصة بشرط قيام صفات مخصوصة بها فإذا عدمت تلك الصفات ، فقد عدم أحد الأجزاء المقمومة لماهية ذلك الإنسان المعين ، وحينئذ يحصل الجزم بأن ذلك الإنسان قد عدم وفني . ثم عند هذا نقول : إن كانت إعادة المعدوم ممكنة ، فلا حاجة إلى القول ببقاء تلك الذوات ، وإن كانت غير ممكنة لم يكن القول ببقاء تلك الذوات كافياً في القول بصحة عود ذلك الإنسان بعينه
As for the people of tradition and transmission, they believed that there is no reality for man except this physical structure. They heard from the prophets and scholars the affirmation of the resurrection and the standing, so they believed both statements, but their thoughts did not reach whether combining these two statements is possible or not. Then a group of mutakallimūn, when they wanted to combine these two statements, mentioned two methods regarding it: First: Some said that the re-creation of the non-existent exactly as it was is permissible. Second: Others said that it is impossible, except that when a man dies, those parts or those essences do not perish; rather, the temperament and attributes perish. So if composition and temperament are restored to those parts, then the returning man is the very same man who existed in the world. This is extremely far-fetched, for we say: The specific man is either an expression of those parts however they are, or the very same with the condition that they are described with specific attributes. The first is false because those parts, when they were devoid of knowledge, life, power, temperament, and composition, were existing, while this man [was not existing. So when this man dies and these attributes cease, those parts remain while that man may not remain. This indicates that this man] is not an expression of those parts [however they are; rather, if it must be, the specific man is an expression of those parts] specific with the condition that specific attributes are established in them. So when those attributes cease, one of the components of the essence of that specific man has ceased, and then certainty is obtained that that man has ceased and perished. Then we say: If the re-creation of the non-existent is possible, then there is no need for the affirmation of the survival of those essences; and if it is not possible, then the affirmation of the survival of those essences is not sufficient for affirming the soundness of the return of that very same man.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the common belief that man is merely the physical body. The author explains that traditionalists (ahl al-taqlīd wa al-athar) accepted both that man is only this body and that there will be a resurrection, without considering whether these two can be reconciled. Mutakallimūn proposed two solutions: first, that God can create the non-existent exactly as it was (iʿādat al-maʿdūm bi ʿaynihi); second, that the parts (ajzāʾ) or essences (dhawāt) never perish, only the temperament and attributes do, so restoring composition to the same parts brings back the same man. The author rejects both as far-fetched. He argues that the specific human (al-insān al-muʿayyan) must be defined either by the parts irrespective of attributes, or by the parts conditioned by specific attributes. The first is false because the parts can exist without the attributes (like life, knowledge) that characterize the living man; thus the parts alone do not constitute the man. So the second definition is forced: the specific man is the parts with specific attributes. When those attributes vanish, one component of the essence is lost, and the man is destroyed. Then the author concludes: if re-creation of the non-existent is possible, the survival of parts is unnecessary; if it is impossible, survival of parts is insufficient for the return of the same man. The passage thus shows the theological dilemma of personal identity in resurrection.

Key Terms
الإنسان المعين
الأجزاء
المزاج والصفات
إعادة المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The survival of the same bodily parts guarantees that the same person returns.
Correction: The author argues that without specific attributes, the parts alone do not constitute the same person.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the first definition of the specific human (as parts however they are)?
A) Because the parts are not real.
B) Because the parts can exist without the attributes that characterize the living human.
C) Because the attributes are more important than the parts.
D) Because the parts are not the same after death.

Key Takeaway
Personal identity in resurrection requires more than the same material parts; it requires the specific attributes that define the living person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your identity is not simply your body but the specific attributes that make you who you are—your life, knowledge, and character—what will remain of 'you' when those fade at death? How does this realization change the way you live today?
?
Sanusi shows that even if your body parts survive, you as a person perishes when your defining attributes vanish. Which attributes of yours do you think will matter most when you stand before Allah? Are you cultivating them now?
