The First Proof: The Soul's Independence from the Body
Sanusi presents the first proof for the soul's survival after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first argument for the survival of the soul, which relies on the soul's nature as a simple substance independent of the body's spatial and causal conditions.

Core Idea
The soul does not die with the body because it is not inside the body.

Before presenting his argument, Sanusi reviews two views about human nature. He then explains that if the soul is a simple substance unrelated to the body except as its sphere of action, then bodily decay does not imply its annihilation. This lesson walks through that reasoning and the necessary knowledge it invokes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two views of human nature
The passage begins by distinguishing those who define man as a body with a specific temperament from those who define man as a simple substance distinct from the body. Note that the second group all agree on the soul's survival.
This sets up the proof.
2
Grasp the nature of the rational soul
The proof asserts the soul is neither a body nor located in a body, and has no spatial relations. This is crucial for the independence argument.
Contrast with common intuition that soul is 'in' the body.
3
Examine the analogy of the ruler and his palace
The soul relates to the body as a ruler to his palace. The ruin of the palace does not require the death of the ruler. Similarly, the body's death does not necessitate the soul's death.
This is presented as necessary knowledge.
4
Identify the source of doubt
Doubt arises from the estimative faculty (al-wahm) wrongly assuming the soul indwells and permeates the body. Once that false assumption is removed, the conclusion becomes clear.
The author distinguishes intellectual insight from imaginative illusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تفصيل قول من يقول : الإنسان عبارة عن الجسم الموصوف بالمزاج المخصوص . وأما الذين يقولون : الإنسان عبارة عن جوهر مجرد مغاير لهذا البدن . فالكل أطبقوا على أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن [ ولما بينا بالدليل : أن النفس جوهر مجرد ، كان قولنا : النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ] مفرعاً على هذا الأصل [ والله أعلم ] . واعلم : أن هذه المسألة لما كانت أهم المهمات ، لا جرم عزمنا على أن تذكر فيها كل ما يمكن ذكره . سواء كان من الوجوه البرهانية ، أو من الوجوه الإقناعية : فالحجة الأولى على بقاء النفس بعد موت البدن : أن نقول : قد ثبت أن النفس الناطقة : جوهر ليس بجسم ولا في جسم البتة . ويمتنع أن يحصل بينه وبين الأجسام قرب أو بعد أو مناسبة. وليس بينها وبين هذا البدن ، إلا أن هذا البدن محل تصرف ذلك الجوهر في منزل عمله ودار مملكته . كما أن الرجل المعين يكون ساكناً في دار ومتصرفاً فيها ، فإذا خرجت تلك الدار وخرجت عن الصلاح والسداد ، لم يلزم موت ذلك المتصرف وبطلانه . وهذا يجري مجرى العلوم الضرورية . والشك والشبهة إنما يتولد بسبب أن الوهم يسبق إلى أن ذلك الشيء حال في هذا البدن ، وساري فيه . فيسبق حينئذ إلى الوهم أنه يجب موتها بموت البدن . وأما إن قدرنا جوهراً مجرداً ليس بينه وبين هذا البدن مناسبة ، لا بالقرب ولا بالبعد ، ولا بوجه من وجوه المناسبة . إلا من وجه واحد ، وهو أن ذلك البدن كان دار مملكة ذلك الجوهر ومحل تصرفه، ثم إنه خربت هذه الدار. فالعلم الضروري حاصل بأن هذا القدر لا يقتضي عدم ذلك الجوهر ، ولا يغير من أحواله ) فهذا لبيان أنه لا يلزم من موت البدن موته حالا
This is the detailed account of the one who says that man is an expression of the body described with the specific temperament. As for those who say that man is an expression of a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) different from this body, they all agreed that the soul remains after the death of the body. [And since we have demonstrated by proof that the soul is a simple substance, our statement that the soul remains after the death of the body] is based on this principle. [And God knows best.] Know that since this matter is the most important of important matters, we have resolved to mention in it everything that can be mentioned, whether from demonstrative proofs (wujūh burhāniyya) or from persuasive proofs (wujūh iqnāʿiyya). The first proof for the survival of the soul after the death of the body is that we say: It has been established that the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) is a substance that is neither a body nor in a body at all. It is impossible for proximity, distance, or relationship to occur between it and bodies. There is no relationship between it and this body except that this body is the place of that substance's activity, the residence of its work, and the abode of its kingdom. Just as a specific man lives in a house and acts in it, if that house falls into ruin and becomes unsound, the death and nullification of that agent does not necessarily follow. This is like necessary knowledge. Doubt and skepticism only arise because the estimative faculty (al-wahm) leaps to the conclusion that that thing is indwelling in the body and permeating it, and thus it leaps to the conclusion that its death is necessary with the death of the body. But if we suppose a simple substance with no relationship to this body, neither in proximity nor distance nor any manner of relationship, except for one aspect, namely that the body was the abode of that substance's kingdom and the place of its activity, and then this house is ruined, then necessary knowledge is obtained that this circumstance does not necessitate the non-existence of that substance nor change its states. This is to show that the death of the body does not necessitate its death instantly.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the survival of the soul after death, following the classification of views about human nature. The author first summarizes the previous view (man as body with temperament) and then turns to the second view: man is a simple, immaterial substance (jawhar mujarrad) distinct from the body. All who hold this view agree that the soul persists after bodily death. The author then states that this conclusion is based on his earlier demonstration that the soul is a simple substance. He announces that, given the importance of this topic, he will present both demonstrative and persuasive arguments. The first proof begins: The rational soul is a substance that is neither a body nor located in a body. There can be no spatial relation (proximity or distance) or natural connection between it and bodies. The only connection is that the body serves as the arena of the soul's activity, like a ruler's palace. Just as the death of the ruler does not follow from the ruin of his palace—this is self-evident (necessarily known)—so too the soul's destruction does not follow from the body's death. The author attributes common doubt to the imaginal faculty (al-wahm) wrongly assuming that the soul is incarnated in the body and thereby must perish with it. Once we correctly understand the soul's separate, non-spatial nature, we see that the soul's existence is independent of the body, and the body's decay does not necessitate the soul's annihilation. This proof relies on the necessary knowledge of the independence of an agent from its instrument.

Key Terms
جوهر مجرد
النفس الناطقة
البدن
الوهم
العلوم الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is inside the body and therefore must die when the body dies.
Correction: The soul is a simple substance not spatially located in the body; its connection is only as an agent to its instrument, so bodily death does not entail its annihilation.

Checkpoint
What is the only relationship between the rational soul and the body according to the first proof?
A) The soul is inside the body and permeates it.
B) The body is the abode and workplace of the soul's activity.
C) The soul is distant from the body but occasionally enters it.
D) There is no relationship at all.

Key Takeaway
The soul's survival after death is established by its nature as a simple substance independent of the body's spatial and material conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine your own death, do you truly believe that your soul will survive independent of your body? Or do you find yourself still thinking of your self as tied to this physical form? What evidence from this lesson can help you overcome that doubt?
?
Sanusi says that the soul's relationship to the body is like a man to his house. If your house were destroyed, would you cease to exist? Then why do you fear death as if it ends your existence? What does this tell you about the strength of your conviction in the afterlife?
?
The estimative faculty (wahm) leads many to mistakenly think the soul dies with the body. Have you ever caught yourself falling into that same error? How can you train your intellect to rely on necessary knowledge rather than imagination when contemplating your mortality?

The Fourth Proof: Gradation of Existence
Al-Razi states the proof and elaborates the first premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the overall structure of the fourth proof and the first premise that accidents are weaker than bodies.

Core Idea
Strength of existence determines independence: accidents need bodies, bodies need nothing physical.

Al-Razi now presents a new proof for the soul's immortality, built on a simple hierarchy: accidents rely on bodies, bodies rely on nothing corporeal, and the soul—being even higher—is unaffected by bodily death. Before we dive into the details, let's grasp the overall argument and its first building block.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's main claim
Read the opening lines where Al-Razi states the conclusion: the soul does not die with the body. Notice the chain of comparison.
2
Understand the three-termed comparison
Identify the three entities compared: accidents, bodies, and the abstract substance (soul). Recognize that each is weaker than the next.
3
Study the first premise in detail
Focus on the first premise: accidents are weaker than bodies. Al-Razi gives a nearly self-evident reason—accidents depend on bodies for existence, but bodies are self-subsistent.
This premise sets the foundation for the hierarchy.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If accidents are weaker than bodies, and bodies are weaker than the soul, what does that mean for the soul's vulnerability? This prepares you for the later premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن : أن الأعراض القائمة بالأجسام، أضعف وجوداً من تلك الأجسام . والأجسام أضعف وجوداً من الموجود المجرد القائم بالنفس ، فيلزم أن تكون الأعراض أضعف بكثير من الموجود المجرد . وإذا كان كذلك ، وجب القطع بأن موت البدن لا يوجب موت النفس . فتفتقر ههنا إلى بيان مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : إن الأعراض القائمة بالأجسام، أضعف في الوجود من تلك الأجسام . وهذا يقرب من أن يكون بديهياً ، لأن الأعراض محتاجة إلى تلك الأجسام ، [ ويمتنع دخولها في الوجود ، إلا في تلك الأجسام ] وأما تلك الأجسام فإنها قائمة بأنفسها وغنية عن تلك الأعراض . وهذا يوجب القطع بأن الأعراض أضعف وجوداً من الأجسام
·
وأما المقدمة الثانية في بيان أن الأجسام أضعف وجوداً من الجوهر المجرد : فبيانها من وجوه :
الأول : إن المتحيزات محتاجة إلى الأمكنة والأحياز والجهات ، والجوهر المجرد غني عنها
·
والثاني : إن كل جسم فهو مركب من [ جزءين ، فصاعداً ] وكل مركب فإنه يحتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، والجوهر المجرد لا يجب أن يكون منقسماً إلى جزئين . فكان هذا النوع من الحاجة زائلاً عن الجوهر المجرد
الثالث : إن النفس تفعل في البدن . أما البدن فإنه لا يفعل البتة في النفس أثراً . والفعل يناسب القوة ، والانفعال يناسب الضعف
الرابع : إن الأجسام متماثلة في ذواتها ، وفي تمام ماهياتها . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون لها أثر في الغير. فإن أثر جسم في جسم فذلك التأثير إنما كان لأجل الأعراض القائمة به. وقد عرفت : أن أضعف المراتب هو الأعراض ، فلما كان تأثير الجسم في غيره بواسطة الأعراض ، وجب أن يكون تأثير الجسم في غيره تأثيراً ضعيفاً . وأما تأثيرات النفوس ، فهي تأثيرات قوية ، قاهرة فوجب أن تكون النفس أقوى من الجسم
الخامس : إن البدن بعد مفارقة النفس ، بقي ما فيه من الجسم وما فيه من العرض . ثم إنه يصير بعد الموت فاسداً باطلا عفناً . وأما حال تعلق النفس بالبدن ، فإنه بقيت تلك الأجزاء مصونة عن العفونة والفساد ، موصوفة بالنقاء والنظافة والطهارة . فثبت بما ذكرناه : أن النفس أقوى حالاً من الجسم ، وأن الجسم أقوى حالاً من العرض. وهذا يدل على أنه لا مناسبة لقوة النفس إلى قوة العرض الحال في الجسم بوجه من الوجوه
المقدمة الثالثة وهي في بيان أن هذا الاعتبار يدل على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن :
والدليل عليه : إن موت البدن ليس عبارة عن فناء تلك الأجسام ، فإنها باقية بتمامها ، وإنما موت البدن عبارة عن زوال الأعراض التي كانت قائمة به حال اعتدال المزاج قبل موتها وفنائها . والمزاج المعتدل عبارة عن كيفية
The fourth proof: that the soul does not die with the death of the body: that the accidents subsisting in bodies are weaker in existence than those bodies. And bodies are weaker in existence than the abstract existent subsisting by itself, which is the soul. Therefore it follows that accidents are much weaker than the abstract existent. And if that is so, it is necessary to decide that the death of the body does not necessitate the death of the soul. So here we need to explain premises:
The first premise: that the accidents subsisting in bodies are weaker in existence than those bodies. And this is close to being self-evident, because accidents need those bodies, [and it is impossible for them to enter existence except in those bodies]. As for those bodies, they subsist by themselves and are independent of those accidents. And this necessitates the decision that accidents are weaker in existence than bodies.
As for the second premise, in explaining that bodies are weaker in existence than the abstract substance: its explanation is from aspects:
First: that spatial entities need places, spaces, and directions, while the abstract substance is independent of them.
Second: that every body is composed of [two parts, upwards] and every composite needs each one of its parts, while the abstract substance is not necessarily divisible into two parts. So this kind of need is absent from the abstract substance.
Third: that the soul acts upon the body. As for the body, it does not act at all upon the soul in any effect. Action befits strength, and passivity befits weakness.
Fourth: that bodies are similar in their essences and in their complete quiddities. And if that is so, it is impossible for them to have an effect upon another. For if a body affects a body, that effect is only due to the accidents subsisting in it. And you have known that the weakest rank is accidents. Since the effect of the body upon another is through accidents, it is necessary that the effect of the body upon another is a weak effect. As for the effects of souls, they are strong, overwhelming effects. So it is necessary that the soul is stronger than the body.
Fifth: that the body after the departure of the soul remains with what is in it of body and what is in it of accident. Then it becomes after death rotten, futile, putrid. As for the state of the soul's attachment to the body, those parts remain preserved from putrefaction and corruption, described with purity, cleanliness, and sanctity. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the soul is stronger in state than the body, and that the body is stronger in state than the accident. And this indicates that there is no proportion between the strength of the soul and the strength of the accident subsisting in the body in any way whatsoever.
The third premise, which is in explaining that this consideration indicates that the soul does not die with the death of the body:
And the proof of it: that the death of the body is not an expression of the annihilation of those bodies, for they remain in their entirety. Rather, the death of the body is an expression of the removal of the accidents that were subsisting in it in the state of balanced temperament before their death and annihilation. And the balanced temperament is an expression of a quality.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the soul's survival of bodily death, structured around three premises. The proof begins by stating that accidents are weaker than bodies, bodies are weaker than the abstract substance (the soul), so accidents are much weaker than the soul. Therefore, bodily death does not entail the soul's death. The author then elaborates the three premises.

The first premise: Accidents are weaker than bodies because accidents depend on bodies for existence, while bodies are self-subsistent and independent of accidents. This is nearly self-evident.

The second premise: Bodies are weaker than the abstract substance (the soul). This is defended through five arguments:
1. Spatial entities (bodies) need places and directions; the abstract substance does not.
2. Bodies are composite, needing their parts; the abstract substance is indivisible and has no such need.
3. The soul acts on the body, but the body never acts on the soul; action indicates strength, passivity weakness.
4. Bodies are identical in essence, so they cannot affect each other except through accidents, which are the weakest. Soul's effects are strong, so the soul is stronger.
5. After the soul leaves, the body decays; while the soul is present, the body is preserved. This shows the soul's superiority.

Thus, bodies are weaker than the soul, and accidents weaker than bodies.

The third premise: This gradation proves the soul does not die with the body. Death is not the annihilation of the body's matter (which remains) but the loss of accidents (like balanced temperament). Since the soul is far stronger than those accidents, their removal does not affect the soul. The argument concludes that there is no proportion between the soul's strength and the weakness of bodily accidents, so the soul survives bodily death.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs)
البدن (al-badan)
الأعراض (al-aʿrāḍ)
الموجود المجرد (al-mawjūd al-mujarrad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims the soul is immortal by its very essence, without need for God.
Correction: The proof only shows that bodily death does not cause the soul's death due to the soul's superior strength; it does not address whether the soul could be annihilated by God or other causes.

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, why are accidents weaker than bodies?
A) Accidents are made of a weaker substance.
B) Accidents depend on bodies for existence, while bodies are self-subsistent.
C) Bodies are created before accidents.
D) Accidents are temporary by definition.

Key Takeaway
The first premise establishes that accidents are weaker than bodies because accidents depend on bodies, but bodies do not depend on accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is merely a temporary container for your soul, what concrete steps are you taking today to strengthen your soul's existence—its knowledge, character, and connection to its Creator—independent of your physical comforts?
?
Al-Razi argues that accidents (like your fleeting moods and physical sensations) are the weakest level of existence, while your soul is the strongest. Which level are you actually investing your life in: the weakest, the intermediate, or the strongest? Be honest.
?
Since bodily death does not necessitate the death of your soul, are you living as if that is true? Do you fear death more as an end of the body, or as a transition for your soul? What needs to change in your priorities to align with this reality?

Second Premise: Bodies Weaker Than the Soul
Al-Razi enumerates five proofs for the second premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp the five arguments proving that bodies are weaker than the abstract substance (soul).

Core Idea
Bodies need space, composition, and are passive; the soul needs none and is active.

Now we move to the second and most detailed premise: proving that bodies are inferior to the soul. Al-Razi gives five distinct arguments, each highlighting a different aspect of the soul's superiority—from independence from space to the body's post-mortem decay. Let's examine each argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise overview
Read the opening of the second premise where Al-Razi says he will present multiple aspects (wujuh).
2
Examine the first two arguments
Arguments 1 and 2 focus on spatial independence and simplicity: bodies need places and are composite, the soul is neither.
3
Examine arguments 3 and 4
Argument 3 uses action vs. passivity; argument 4 notes that bodies affect only through accidents, while the soul affects directly and strongly.
4
Study the fifth argument
Argument 5 compares the state of the body before and after the soul departs, showing the soul's preserving power.
5
Synthesize the five arguments
Consider how together they establish the soul's greater strength than the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن : أن الأعراض القائمة بالأجسام، أضعف وجوداً من تلك الأجسام . والأجسام أضعف وجوداً من الموجود المجرد القائم بالنفس ، فيلزم أن تكون الأعراض أضعف بكثير من الموجود المجرد . وإذا كان كذلك ، وجب القطع بأن موت البدن لا يوجب موت النفس . فتفتقر ههنا إلى بيان مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : إن الأعراض القائمة بالأجسام، أضعف في الوجود من تلك الأجسام . وهذا يقرب من أن يكون بديهياً ، لأن الأعراض محتاجة إلى تلك الأجسام ، [ ويمتنع دخولها في الوجود ، إلا في تلك الأجسام ] وأما تلك الأجسام فإنها قائمة بأنفسها وغنية عن تلك الأعراض . وهذا يوجب القطع بأن الأعراض أضعف وجوداً من الأجسام
·
وأما المقدمة الثانية في بيان أن الأجسام أضعف وجوداً من الجوهر المجرد : فبيانها من وجوه :
الأول : إن المتحيزات محتاجة إلى الأمكنة والأحياز والجهات ، والجوهر المجرد غني عنها
·
والثاني : إن كل جسم فهو مركب من [ جزءين ، فصاعداً ] وكل مركب فإنه يحتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، والجوهر المجرد لا يجب أن يكون منقسماً إلى جزئين . فكان هذا النوع من الحاجة زائلاً عن الجوهر المجرد
الثالث : إن النفس تفعل في البدن . أما البدن فإنه لا يفعل البتة في النفس أثراً . والفعل يناسب القوة ، والانفعال يناسب الضعف
الرابع : إن الأجسام متماثلة في ذواتها ، وفي تمام ماهياتها . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون لها أثر في الغير. فإن أثر جسم في جسم فذلك التأثير إنما كان لأجل الأعراض القائمة به. وقد عرفت : أن أضعف المراتب هو الأعراض ، فلما كان تأثير الجسم في غيره بواسطة الأعراض ، وجب أن يكون تأثير الجسم في غيره تأثيراً ضعيفاً . وأما تأثيرات النفوس ، فهي تأثيرات قوية ، قاهرة فوجب أن تكون النفس أقوى من الجسم
الخامس : إن البدن بعد مفارقة النفس ، بقي ما فيه من الجسم وما فيه من العرض . ثم إنه يصير بعد الموت فاسداً باطلا عفناً . وأما حال تعلق النفس بالبدن ، فإنه بقيت تلك الأجزاء مصونة عن العفونة والفساد ، موصوفة بالنقاء والنظافة والطهارة . فثبت بما ذكرناه : أن النفس أقوى حالاً من الجسم ، وأن الجسم أقوى حالاً من العرض. وهذا يدل على أنه لا مناسبة لقوة النفس إلى قوة العرض الحال في الجسم بوجه من الوجوه
المقدمة الثالثة وهي في بيان أن هذا الاعتبار يدل على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن :
والدليل عليه : إن موت البدن ليس عبارة عن فناء تلك الأجسام ، فإنها باقية بتمامها ، وإنما موت البدن عبارة عن زوال الأعراض التي كانت قائمة به حال اعتدال المزاج قبل موتها وفنائها . والمزاج المعتدل عبارة عن كيفية
The fourth proof: that the soul does not die with the death of the body: that the accidents subsisting in bodies are weaker in existence than those bodies. And bodies are weaker in existence than the abstract existent subsisting by itself, which is the soul. Therefore it follows that accidents are much weaker than the abstract existent. And if that is so, it is necessary to decide that the death of the body does not necessitate the death of the soul. So here we need to explain premises:
The first premise: that the accidents subsisting in bodies are weaker in existence than those bodies. And this is close to being self-evident, because accidents need those bodies, [and it is impossible for them to enter existence except in those bodies]. As for those bodies, they subsist by themselves and are independent of those accidents. And this necessitates the decision that accidents are weaker in existence than bodies.
As for the second premise, in explaining that bodies are weaker in existence than the abstract substance: its explanation is from aspects:
First: that spatial entities need places, spaces, and directions, while the abstract substance is independent of them.
Second: that every body is composed of [two parts, upwards] and every composite needs each one of its parts, while the abstract substance is not necessarily divisible into two parts. So this kind of need is absent from the abstract substance.
Third: that the soul acts upon the body. As for the body, it does not act at all upon the soul in any effect. Action befits strength, and passivity befits weakness.
Fourth: that bodies are similar in their essences and in their complete quiddities. And if that is so, it is impossible for them to have an effect upon another. For if a body affects a body, that effect is only due to the accidents subsisting in it. And you have known that the weakest rank is accidents. Since the effect of the body upon another is through accidents, it is necessary that the effect of the body upon another is a weak effect. As for the effects of souls, they are strong, overwhelming effects. So it is necessary that the soul is stronger than the body.
Fifth: that the body after the departure of the soul remains with what is in it of body and what is in it of accident. Then it becomes after death rotten, futile, putrid. As for the state of the soul's attachment to the body, those parts remain preserved from putrefaction and corruption, described with purity, cleanliness, and sanctity. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the soul is stronger in state than the body, and that the body is stronger in state than the accident. And this indicates that there is no proportion between the strength of the soul and the strength of the accident subsisting in the body in any way whatsoever.
The third premise, which is in explaining that this consideration indicates that the soul does not die with the death of the body:
And the proof of it: that the death of the body is not an expression of the annihilation of those bodies, for they remain in their entirety. Rather, the death of the body is an expression of the removal of the accidents that were subsisting in it in the state of balanced temperament before their death and annihilation. And the balanced temperament is an expression of a quality.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the soul's survival of bodily death, structured around three premises. The proof begins by stating that accidents are weaker than bodies, bodies are weaker than the abstract substance (the soul), so accidents are much weaker than the soul. Therefore, bodily death does not entail the soul's death. The author then elaborates the three premises.

The first premise: Accidents are weaker than bodies because accidents depend on bodies for existence, while bodies are self-subsistent and independent of accidents. This is nearly self-evident.

The second premise: Bodies are weaker than the abstract substance (the soul). This is defended through five arguments:
1. Spatial entities (bodies) need places and directions; the abstract substance does not.
2. Bodies are composite, needing their parts; the abstract substance is indivisible and has no such need.
3. The soul acts on the body, but the body never acts on the soul; action indicates strength, passivity weakness.
4. Bodies are identical in essence, so they cannot affect each other except through accidents, which are the weakest. Soul's effects are strong, so the soul is stronger.
5. After the soul leaves, the body decays; while the soul is present, the body is preserved. This shows the soul's superiority.

Thus, bodies are weaker than the soul, and accidents weaker than bodies.

The third premise: This gradation proves the soul does not die with the body. Death is not the annihilation of the body's matter (which remains) but the loss of accidents (like balanced temperament). Since the soul is far stronger than those accidents, their removal does not affect the soul. The argument concludes that there is no proportion between the soul's strength and the weakness of bodily accidents, so the soul survives bodily death.

Key Terms
الجوهر المجرد (al-jawhar al-mujarrad)
المتحيزات (al-mutaḥayyizāt)
المركب (al-murakkab)
الانفعال (al-infʿāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is stronger because it is immaterial, but that does not make it more 'real' or effective.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that immateriality actually grants greater independence and causal power, as shown by the soul's ability to act on and preserve the body.

Checkpoint
Which argument uses the fact that bodies are alike in essence to prove they cannot affect each other directly?
A) Argument 1
B) Argument 2
C) Argument 3
D) Argument 4

Key Takeaway
The five arguments collectively show that bodies are far weaker than the soul in terms of independence, simplicity, activity, causal power, and preservative ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is truly independent of space and place, where is your real home? Are you living as if your soul is chained to physical locations, or are you cultivating its independence from material attachments? What step will you take today to act from your soul's strength rather than being passively driven by your body's desires?
?
The body without the soul decays. What parts of your spiritual life are decaying because you have neglected to attach them to your soul's purpose? Name one habit that you need to resurrect by reconnecting it to your intention for Allah.
?
The soul acts upon the body but the body does not act upon the soul. In your daily life, are you letting your physical circumstances dictate your inner state, or are you proactively shaping your character and faith regardless of your situation? Choose one internal quality you will cultivate starting tomorrow simply because you decide to, not because your body feels like it.

Third Premise: Conclusion of the Proof
Al-Razi presents the third premise and concludes the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third premise uses the hierarchy to prove that the soul does not die with the body.

Core Idea
Death is loss of accidents, not matter; the soul, being stronger, remains unaffected.

After establishing the hierarchy of strength, Al-Razi now draws the final conclusion. He clarifies that death is not the annihilation of the body's matter but the removal of certain accidents (like balanced temperament). Since the soul is incomparably stronger than any accident, its departure does not cause the soul's death. Let's examine this final step.

Guided Path
1
Read the third premise
Read how Al-Razi defines death as the removal of accidents, not the destruction of the body's substance.
2
Connect to the earlier hierarchy
Recall that the soul is much stronger than any accident. How does that relate to the nature of death?
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Since death only removes weak accidents, the strong soul is not affected. Therefore, the soul outlives the body's death.
4
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how the three premises work together to form a complete syllogism.

And bodies are weaker in existence than the abstract existent subsisting by itself, which is the soul. Therefore it follows that accidents are much weaker than the abstract existent. Commentary

Second Proof: The Soul Is Not Composite Like Bodies
The author presents the second proof that the soul is not composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that the soul is not a composite body and how this distinguishes the soul from physical substances.

Core Idea
The soul is not a body because bodies need parts; the soul needs none.

This lesson presents the second proof from the text, which argues that the soul cannot be a composite body. The author uses a simple logical contrast: every body is composed of parts and depends on them, but the soul—as a simple substance—does not require parts. This distinction clarifies the soul's non-physical nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to grasp the logical flow.
2
Identify the key premises
Note the two premises: bodies are composite, composites need parts.
3
See the conclusion
The soul is not like a body; it lacks that need.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that this argument shows the soul is a simple substance, not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن كل جسم فهو مركب من [ جزءين ، فصاعداً ] وكل مركب فإنه يحتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، والجوهر المجرد لا يجب أن يكون منقسماً إلى جزئين . فكان هذا النوع من الحاجة زائلاً عن الجوهر المجرد
And the second (argument): Every body is composed of [ two parts, or more ] and every composite needs each one of its parts, and the simple substance (al-jawhar al-mujarrad) is not necessarily divisible into two parts. So this kind of need is absent from the simple substance.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument proving the soul's nature. The author argues that every body is composite, consisting of at least two parts. Any composite entity requires each of its parts for its existence. However, the simple substance (al-jawhar al-mujarrad)—which refers to the soul—is not necessarily divisible into parts. Therefore, the type of need that characterizes bodies does not apply to the soul, indicating that the soul is not a body and is of a different nature.

Key Terms
الجسم
الجوهر المجرد
المركب
الجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'simple substance' means the soul is a body but indivisible.
Correction: The author argues that the soul is not a body at all because bodies are composite and need parts, whereas the soul is simple and lacks that need.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why is the soul not a body?
A) Because bodies are composite and need parts, but the soul is simple and does not need parts.
B) Because the soul is invisible.
C) Because bodies are material.
D) Because the soul is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The soul is not a composite body because it does not depend on parts, unlike all physical bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is not composed of parts like your body, then your true identity is something indivisible and eternal. What does that mean for how you view yourself when your body ages or changes? How can you focus on nurturing your soul instead of your body today?
?
The proof shows that the soul does not need parts, but it still needs its Creator. In what ways do you mistakenly act as if your soul is self-sufficient, ignoring your dependence on Allah for your spiritual life? Commit to one act of humility today that acknowledges your need for Him.
?

Fourth Proof: Bodies Weak, Souls Strong
The author argues that bodies are similar and weak, while souls have strong effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that bodies cannot have strong effects and why this points to the soul's superior strength.

Core Idea
Bodies are identical in essence; only accidents give them weak effects.

This argument contrasts the inherent weakness of bodies with the powerful effects of souls. The author first establishes that bodies are essentially similar and thus cannot affect each other on their own. Any effect must come through accidents, which are the weakest form of existence, leading to a necessary conclusion about the soul's strength.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice how the author starts with a premise about bodies.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: (1) bodies are similar, (2) no inherent effect, (3) effect only via accidents, (4) accidents are weakest, (5) body's effect weak, (6) soul's effect strong, (7) soul stronger.
This is a clear chain of reasoning.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'أجسام' (bodies), 'أعراض' (accidents), and 'نفوس' (souls). Know their definitions in this context.
Accidents are temporary attributes, unlike essences.
4

Ask: Why does this argument support the existence of a powerful Creator? The soul's strength points to a higher cause.
This connects to the broader theme of divine existence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن الأجسام متماثلة في ذواتها ، وفي تمام ماهياتها . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون لها أثر في الغير. فإن أثر جسم في جسم فذلك التأثير إنما كان لأجل الأعراض القائمة به. وقد عرفت : أن أضعف المراتب هو الأعراض ، فلما كان تأثير الجسم في غيره بواسطة الأعراض ، وجب أن يكون تأثير الجسم في غيره تأثيراً ضعيفاً . وأما تأثيرات النفوس ، فهي تأثيرات قوية ، قاهرة فوجب أن تكون النفس أقوى من الجسم

So if one body affects another body, that effect is only due to the accidents subsisting in it. And you have known that the weakest rank is accidents. So since the body's effect upon another is through accidents, the effect of the body upon another must be a weak effect. As for the effects of souls, they are strong, overwhelming effects. So the soul must be stronger than the body.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. The author begins by stating that bodies are identical (mutamāthila) in their essences and quiddities. Consequently, the body's effects are weak. In contrast, the effects of souls are strong and overwhelming. Therefore, the soul is necessarily stronger than the body.

Fifth Proof: Body Preserved by Soul, Decays After
Al-Razi contrasts the body's states with and without the soul to argue for the soul's strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the preservation of the body during life and its decay after death demonstrates the soul's superior strength as a proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
The soul's power is shown by how it keeps the body intact while present.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from a classical theological work. The author uses a simple observation: when the soul is with the body, the body remains pure and preserved; after separation, it decays. This contrast reveals the soul's strength, which is incomparable to any physical accident. By understanding this argument, you will see how material phenomena point to a non-material reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Focus on the two contrasting states described.
The passage is short but dense; note the sequence of observations.
2
Identify the two states and their outcomes
Notice the author contrasts the body's state after the soul leaves (decays) with its state while the soul is attached (preserved).
3
Understand the hierarchy of strength
The author concludes a strength hierarchy: soul > body > accident. Reflect on why the body is stronger than an accident and the soul stronger than the body.
This hierarchy is based on observable durability.
4
Consider the implication
The author states the soul's strength is incomparable to that of an accident. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the soul's nature and its origin?
This leads to the conclusion that the soul is not a mere accident and points to a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن البدن بعد مفارقة النفس ، بقي ما فيه من الجسم وما فيه من العرض . ثم إنه يصير بعد الموت فاسداً باطلا عفناً . وأما حال تعلق النفس بالبدن ، فإنه بقيت تلك الأجزاء مصونة عن العفونة والفساد ، موصوفة بالنقاء والنظافة والطهارة . فثبت بما ذكرناه : أن النفس أقوى حالاً من الجسم ، وأن الجسم أقوى حالاً من العرض. وهذا يدل على أنه لا مناسبة لقوة النفس إلى قوة العرض الحال في الجسم بوجه من الوجوه
The fifth (argument): When the soul leaves the body, what remains of the body and what remains of the accident persist. Then after death it becomes corrupt, null, and decayed. As for the state of the soul's attachment to the body, those parts remained protected from decay and corruption, characterized by purity, cleanliness, and cleanliness. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the soul is stronger in state than the body, and the body is stronger in state than the accident. And this indicates that there is no comparison between the strength of the soul and the strength of the accident subsisting in the body in any way.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument. The author observes that after the soul leaves the body, the body and its accidents (like color) remain initially, but then the body decays and becomes corrupt. However, while the soul is attached, the body's parts are protected from decay and are characterized by purity and cleanliness. From this, the author concludes that the soul is stronger in state than the body, and the body is stronger than the accident. Thus, there is no comparison between the soul's strength and that of an accident residing in the body.

The Soul Does Not Die with the Body – Proof from the Nature of Death
The author defines death and argues for the soul's immortality based on relative strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author redefines death as the loss of bodily accidents and uses the relative strength of accidents and souls to prove the soul's immortality.

Core Idea
Death is not annihilation—it is the loss of accidents, and the soul is too strong to fade.

In this third introduction, the author presents a clever argument: physical death is not the end of the body's substance, but merely the disappearance of certain accidental qualities. By showing that accidents are the weakest possible beings while the soul is the strongest, he concludes that the soul cannot cease to exist when the body dies. This reframing of death itself is key to the proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thesis
The passage aims to prove that the soul does not die with the body. Pay attention to how the author redefines death to make this case.
The thesis is stated at the beginning.
2
Grasp the redefinition of death
Death is not annihilation of body substance; rather, it is the disappearance of accidents like balanced temperament. Note the reasoning: the body persists materially.
The author uses an example: balanced temperament is an accidental quality.
3
Notice the classification of creation
The author contrasts accidents (weakest possible things) with simple souls (strongest possible things). This classification is crucial for the logical step.
This is a key premise from prior discussions.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
Given that the strongest possible thing cannot cease to exist (by losing something weaker), the soul must survive bodily death. See how the author states this yields certainty.
The argument relies on necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة وهي في بيان أن هذا الاعتبار يدل على أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن : والدليل عليه : إن موت البدن ليس عبارة عن فناء تلك الأجسام ، فإنها باقية بتمامها ، وإنما موت البدن عبارة عن زوال الأعراض التي كانت قائمة به حال اعتدال المزاج قبل موتها وفنائها . والمزاج المعتدل عبارة عن كيفية متوسطة بين الأضداد ، فهو عرض مخصوص . فإذا ماتت ، فلا الموت إلا زوال هذه الكيفية. فثبت : أنه لا معنى للموت إلا زوال بعض أنواع الأعراض عن الأجسام المخصوصة . وقد دللنا على أن الأعراض أضعف أقسام الممكنات [ وعلى أن النفوس المجردة أقوى أقسام الممكنات ] والعلم الضروري حاصل بأن زوال الشيء الذي يكون وجوده في غاية القوة . وهذا يفيد الجزم بأن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن .
The third introduction, which is concerning the death of the body: an explanation that this consideration indicates that the soul does not die with the death of the body. The proof of it: The death of the body is not the annihilation of those bodies, for they persist in their entirety. Rather, the death of the body is the disappearance of the accidents that subsisted in it during the state of balanced temperament before their death and annihilation. Balanced temperament is a quality intermediate between opposites, so it is a specific accident. So when they (the bodies) die, death is nothing but the disappearance of this quality. So it is established that there is no meaning to death except the disappearance of some kinds of accidents from specific bodies. And we have demonstrated that accidents are the weakest of the divisions of possibles, and that simple souls are the strongest of the divisions of possibles. And necessary knowledge obtains that the disappearance of a thing whose existence is extremely strong is impossible. And this yields certainty that the soul does not die with the death of the body.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is the third introduction, proving that the soul does not die when the body dies. The author redefines death: it is not the annihilation of the body's material, which remains, but rather the disappearance of specific accidental qualities, such as the balanced temperament (al-mizāj al-muʿtadil). This balanced temperament is a quality between extremes—a specific accident. Thus, death is the loss of accidents. Since accidents are the weakest of possible things, and the soul (simple substance) is the strongest, it is necessarily known that the strongest thing cannot cease to exist. Therefore, the soul does not die with the body's death.

Fifth Proof: Soul Doesn't Get Sick, So Doesn't Die
The author argues that since the soul does not become sick, it will not die.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that the soul does not die with the body and the initial argument that the soul does not become sick.

Core Idea
The soul resists disease, so it does not get sick and will not die.

This lesson presents the fifth proof for the soul's immortality. The author uses medical reasoning to show that the soul is distinct from the body and unaffected by its illnesses. By examining the concept of a 'crisis' in illness, he argues that the resisting nature must be the soul, not the body's temperament.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Notice the structure: a claim, then a supporting argument with a refutation of an alternative.
The passage is cut off at the end; the second refutation is incomplete.
2
Identify the main premise
Find the key sentence: 'The soul does not fall sick with the sickness of the body, so it must not die with the death of the body.' This is the core claim.
3
Analyze the medical argument
Understand the concept of 'crisis' (al-buhran) as resistance between nature and disease. The author then considers what this nature could be: temperament, quality, or soul.
The first alternative (temperament) is refuted with two reasons, but only the first reason is fully given.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Note that the passage is truncated. The second reason for rejecting the temperament is cut off. Consider how the argument would continue based on the pattern.
This shows the importance of complete texts in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في بيان أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن : نقول : إن النفس لا تمرض بمرض البدن ، فوجب أن لا تموت بموت البدن . إنما قلنا : إنها لا تمرض بمرض البدن ، وذلك لأن الشواهد الطبية دالة على أنه لا معنى للبحران إلا حصول المقاومة بين الطبيعة وبين العلة ، فهذه الطبيعة التي تقاوم العلة ، إما أن تكون هي مزاج البدن ، أو كيفية [ من الكيفيات ] القائمة بالبدن ، وإما أن تكون هي النفس . والأول باطل لوجهين : أحدهما : إن جسم البدن موصوف بالكيفيات المرضية المنافية للصحة فلو كان هو بعينه موصوفا بالكيفيات الصحية الملائمة للصحة ، لزم كون الجسم الواحد [ في الوقت الواحد ] موصوفاً بصفتين متضادتين . وهو محال . وثانيهما : إن البدن حصل في حقه أسباب ثلاثة ، كل واحد منها يوجب الضعف . فأحدها : الحرارة الغريزية العرضية ، الموجبة لتحلل الرطوبات الكثيرة . وثانيها : إن الحرارة الغريزية أيضاً موجبة لتحلل الرطوبات على قياس ما كان حال الصحة . وثالثها : إن في وقت البحران تنقطع الطبيعة عن
The fifth proof in explaining that the soul does not die with the death of the body: We say: The soul does not fall sick with the sickness of the body, so it must not die with the death of the body. We only said that it does not fall sick with the sickness of the body because medical evidences indicate that there is no meaning to the crisis (al-buhran) except the occurrence of resistance between the nature (al-tabi'a) and the disease. So this nature that resists the disease is either the temperament of the body, or a quality among the qualities subsisting in the body, or it is the soul. The first is false for two reasons: One: The body's substance is described by pathological qualities contrary to health. So if it itself were described by healthy qualities compatible with health, it would entail one single substance being described by two contradictory qualities at one time, which is impossible. And the second: The body has three causes occurring to it, each of which necessitates weakness. The first: the accidental innate heat that causes the dissolution of many moistures. The second: the innate heat also causes dissolution of moistures analogous to the state of health. The third: at the time of crisis, the nature ceases from ... (text cut off).
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that the soul does not die with the body. The author's major premise is that the soul does not become sick when the body is sick, so it will not die when the body dies. To support that the soul does not get sick, he uses medical evidence: a crisis (buḥrān) involves resistance between nature (ṭabīʿa) and disease. The resisting nature must be either the body's temperament, a quality in the body, or the soul. The first possibility (temperament of the body) is refuted with two reasons. First, the body's substance would then have to possess both pathological and healthy qualities simultaneously, which is impossible. Second, the body has three causes (innate heat causing dissolution, etc.) that all weaken it, implying that if the temperament were the resisting nature, it would be contradictory. The text cuts off before completing the second reason, but the argument establishes that the resisting nature is the soul, which does not get sick, hence cannot die.

The Soul's Two Powers Are Independent of the Body
Sanusi enumerates the soul's two powers and argues each is independent of the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's seventh proof that the soul's theoretical and practical powers are independent of the body, establishing that the soul does not die with the body.

Core Idea
The soul's powers do not depend on the body for their existence or operation.

Sanusi presents his seventh proof for the soul's immortality, based on the soul's two inherent powers. He argues that both the theoretical power (acquiring knowledge) and the practical power (managing the body) are unconditioned by the body. This passage walks through the logical steps, from concept formation to syllogistic reasoning, to show that the soul's essence and attributes are independent of bodily existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two powers
Read the opening lines to find the division of the soul into theoretical and practical powers. Understand their definitions.
The theoretical power acquires concepts and judgments; the practical power manages the body.
2
Analyze the argument for theoretical power
Trace the sequence: concepts, assent, self-evident propositions, syllogisms. Notice that none of these steps require the body.
The author emphasizes that concepts emanate from higher causes directly onto the soul.
3
Analyze the argument for practical power
Understand why the body being a locus of execution does not make it a cause, and how the body's receptivity precludes it from being an agent.
The practical power is independent because the body is merely a tool.
4
Connect to essence independence
See how the independence of powers implies the soul's essence is also independent, and this leads to the conclusion that the soul does not die with the body.
The author promises a separate proof for essence independence but states the conclusion here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة في بيان أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن : هو أن النفس لها قوتان : نظرية : وهي القوة التي باعتبارها تقوى على استفادة العلوم التصورية والتصديقية من عالم المجردات . وقوة عملية : وهي القوة التي باعتبارها تقوى على تدبير هذا البدن ، وعلى التصرف فيه فأما القوة النظرية فهي غير مشروطة البتة بالبدن . وذلك لأن محل التصورات ليس إلا النفس . فإذا حصل في جوهر النفس استعداد لقبول تلك التصورات ، فاضت تلك التصورات عن العلل العالية على جوهر النفس وإذا حصل تصور الموضوع وتصور المحمول كان ذانك التصوران محال يلزم من مجرد حصولهما حصول التصديق . فحينئذ تحصل القضايا البديهية لا محالة . وإذا حصلت القضايا البديهية ، واتفق أن حصل فيها قضيتان مشتركتان في الحد الأوسط فحينئذ يكون حصول هاتين القضيتين في العقل ، موجباً لحصول العلم بتلك النتيجة . ثم إذا انضافت تلك النتيجة إلى نتيجة أخرى على الشرط المذكور ، حصلت نتيجة ثانية. وظاهر أن شيئاً من هذه المراتب والدرجات لا يتوقف على اعتبار حال البدن وجوداً وعدماً . فثبت : أن النفس في قوتها النظرية غنية عن البدن ، وأما قوتها العملية فهي غنية أيضاً عن البدن . وذلك لأن البدن محل لنفاذ تلك القدرة ، وما يكون محلا لنفاذ القدرة ، لا يكون علة لتلك القدرة وأيضاً : البدن قابل للتصرف عن النفس فيمتنع أن يكون فاعلاً في النفس ، لأن القابل من حيث إنه قابل ، لا يكون فاعلاً . فثبت : أن النفس غنية في قوتها النظرية ، وفي قوتها العملية عن البدن . أما أنها غنية في ذاتها عن البدن ، فلأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن المؤثر في وجود الجوهر المجرد ، يمتنع أن يكون جسماً أو حالا في جسم . فثبت بهذا البيان الظاهر : أن النفس غنية في ذاتها وفي صفاتها عن البدن . والغني مطلقاً عن الشيء ، لا يجب فساده عند فساد ذلك الشيء . فوجب الجزم بأن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن ، ولا تضعف بضعفه . وهو المطلوب
The seventh proof explaining that the soul does not die with the death of the body: it is that the soul has two powers: a theoretical power—which is the power by virtue of which it is able to acquire conceptual and assertoric sciences from the world of abstract entities—and a practical power—which is the power by virtue of which it is able to manage this body and to act upon it. As for the theoretical power, it is absolutely not conditioned by the body. That is because the locus of concepts is nothing but the soul. So if in the substance of the soul an aptitude occurs to accept those concepts, those concepts emanate from the higher causes upon the substance of the soul. And if the concept of the subject and the concept of the predicate occur, those two concepts are a matter from whose mere occurrence the occurrence of assent necessarily follows. Then the self-evident propositions occur inevitably. And when the self-evident propositions occur, and it happens that among them there are two propositions sharing the middle term, then the occurrence of these two propositions in the intellect necessitates the occurrence of knowledge of that conclusion. Then if that conclusion is combined with another conclusion under the mentioned condition, a second conclusion occurs. And it is apparent that none of these stages and degrees depends on considering the state of the body, existence or non-existence. So it is established that the soul, in its theoretical power, is independent of the body. As for its practical power, it is also independent of the body. That is because the body is a locus for the execution of that power, and what is a locus for the execution of a power cannot be a cause of that power. Also, the body is receptive to being acted upon by the soul, so it is impossible for it to be an agent upon the soul, because the receptive, insofar as it is receptive, is not an agent. So it is established that the soul is independent in its theoretical power and in its practical power from the body. As for its being independent in its essence from the body, we will soon provide evidence that the one who effects the existence of an abstract substance cannot be a body or a state in a body. So by this clear exposition it is established that the soul is independent in its essence and in its attributes from the body. And that which is absolutely independent of a thing, its corruption is not necessitated when that thing corrupts. So it is necessary to be certain that the soul does not die with the death of the body, nor does it weaken with its weakening. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the seventh) for the soul's survival of bodily death, based on the soul's two powers. The author first divides the soul into a theoretical power (quwwa naẓariyya) and a practical power (quwwa ʿamaliyya). The theoretical power acquires concepts and judgments from the world of abstract entities. The author argues that this power is unconditioned by the body: concepts reside in the soul alone; when the soul has aptitude, concepts emanate from higher causes; from concepts of subject and predicate, assent (taṣdīq) necessarily follows, yielding self-evident propositions; these propositions, when sharing a middle term, produce scientific knowledge; further combinations yield further conclusions. None of this depends on the body's existence or non-existence. Thus the theoretical power is independent. The practical power is also independent, because the body is merely the locus for the soul's power, not its cause. Moreover, the body is receptive to the soul's action, so it cannot be an agent upon the soul. After establishing the soul's independence in its powers, the author notes that the soul's essence is also independent, as will be shown separately (that the cause of an abstract substance cannot be a body or bodily state). Since the soul is absolutely independent of the body, its corruption is not entailed by the body's corruption. Therefore, the soul neither dies nor weakens with the body. The argument proceeds stepwise, distinguishing powers and establishing their independence.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs)
قوة نظرية (quwwa naẓariyya)
قوة عملية (quwwa ʿamaliyya)
تصور (taṣawwur)
تصديق (taṣdīq)
قضايا بديهية (qaḍāyā badīhiyya)
حد أوسط (ḥadd awsaṭ)
جوهر مجرد (jawhar mujarrad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the soul's powers are inherently dependent on the body for their existence and operation.
Correction: The author shows that both theoretical and practical powers operate independently of the body, as argued through logical steps.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the theoretical power of the soul is not conditioned by the body?
A) Because the body is a cause of knowledge
B) Because concepts reside only in the soul, and their acquisition does not require the body
C) Because the soul uses the body as a tool for thinking
D) Because the body is the locus of the practical power only

Key Takeaway
The soul's independence from the body in both its theoretical and practical powers proves that it survives bodily death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul's theoretical power is completely independent of your body, then what physical distractions (like tiredness or discomfort) do you use as excuses to avoid seeking knowledge? Will you commit to overcoming one such excuse this week?
?
Your practical power manages your body, yet you often feel overpowered by bodily desires. Since this power is independent and not caused by the body, why do you let your body dictate your actions? What is one habit you will change to assert your soul's authority over your body?
?
The soul does not weaken with the body's weakness. When you experience physical decline or illness, do you truly believe your spiritual growth is unaffected? How can you actively strengthen your soul even when your body is weak?

Eighth Proof: If the Soul Perished, Reason Would Be a Curse
Sanusi argues from final perfection: if only bodily pleasures exist, reason reduces them, making the intelligent worse off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and force of Sanusi's eighth proof for the soul's survival, which uses a reductio ad absurdum based on the value of reason.

Core Idea
If the soul perishes, reason makes us worse than animals—but reason is our glory.

Sanusi now presents a striking argument: if the soul did not survive death, then reason would be a disadvantage, making rational humans worse off than animals. Since our innate reason rejects that, the soul must survive. This proof relies on the principle that the ultimate end must justify reason's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Notice that the argument begins with a conditional: if the soul does not remain, then... The author then explores the consequence.
3
Work through the reductio
See how the author shows that if only bodily pleasures matter, reason would be a drawback, leading to the absurdity that rational humans are baser than animals.
4
Examine the appeal to innate reason
The author relies on badīhat al-ʿaql—the innate judgment that humans are superior to animals. This is presented as undeniable.
This is the key move that refutes the assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إنا قد ذكرنا في باب إثبات النفس : أنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن لكان أخس الحيوانات هو الإنسان والتالي باطل فالمقدم باطل . بيان الملازمة : إن بتقدير أن لا تكون النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، كان كمال السعادة ونهايتها هو الفوز بهذه اللذات الجسمانية ، والراحات البدنية . وحصول العقل يوجب تنقيص هذه الحالة . وهي تنقيص هذه اللذات ، وتنقيص هذه الراحات والسعادات وأما عدم العقل فإنه يوجب تكميل هذه الراحات، وقوة هذه السعادات. فإذا كان لا كمال ولا سعادة إلا هذه اللذات البدنية والخيرات الجسمية . وكان العقل سبباً لنقصانها تارة ، ولبطلانها أخرى ، وجب أن يكون العقل أخس الصفات وأدونها ، وأن يكون العاقل أخس ممن ليس بعاقل فثبت : أنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية ، لكان الإنسان أخس الحيوانات، لكن التالي باطل فإن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن الإنسان كالملك ، وسائر الحيوانات كالعبيد له . وإن الإنسان العاقل كالملك ، والجهال كالعبيد له وذلك يدل على أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن
The eighth proof: We have already mentioned in the chapter on establishing the soul that if the soul were not remaining after the death of the body, then the basest of animals would be the human, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. Explanation of the implication: On the supposition that the soul does not remain after the death of the body, the perfection and ultimate end of happiness would be attaining these bodily pleasures and physical comforts. And the possession of reason necessitates diminishing this state—diminishing these pleasures, and diminishing these comforts and felicities. As for the absence of reason, it necessitates perfecting these comforts and strengthening these felicities. So if there is no perfection or happiness except these bodily pleasures and physical goods, and reason is a cause of their diminution sometimes and their nullification other times, then reason must be the basest and lowest of attributes, and the rational person must be baser than one who is not rational. So it is established that if the soul were not remaining, the human would be the basest of animals, but the consequent is false, for the innate judgment of reason rules that the human is like a king and the rest of animals are like slaves to him, and that the rational human is like a king and the ignorant are like slaves to him. And that indicates that the soul remains after the death of the body.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the eighth) for the soul's survival, based on a reductio ad absurdum. The author assumes for the sake of argument that the soul does not survive death. If that were the case, the ultimate happiness would be purely bodily pleasures and comforts. Reason would then diminish these pleasures (by reflection, moral restraint, etc.), while lack of reason would enhance them. Therefore, reason would be an inferior attribute, and rational humans would be worse off than irrational animals. But innate reason (badīhat al-ʿaql) judges that humans, especially rational ones, are superior to animals—humans are like kings, animals like slaves. This contradicts the conclusion, so the assumption that the soul does not survive must be false. Thus the soul remains after death. The argument uses a conditional: if no afterlife, then reason is disadvantageous; but reason is advantageous (as per universal intuition); therefore there must be an afterlife. The author emphasizes that the intuitive judgment of reason itself refutes the premise.

Key Terms
بقاء النفس (baqā' al-nafs)
السعادة (saʿāda)
العقل (ʿaql)
بديهة العقل (badīhat al-ʿaql)
أخس الحيوانات (akhass al-ḥayawānāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes animals are inferior, making it circular.
Correction: The argument does not assume; it appeals to a universal innate judgment that humans are superior, which the opponent already accepts.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude if the soul were not to survive death?
A) Humans would be the noblest of animals
B) Reason would be the highest attribute
C) Humans would be the basest of animals
D) Bodily pleasures would be diminished

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's eighth proof shows that without an afterlife, reason would be a curse, contradicting our innate conviction that reason elevates humanity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If, hypothetically, there were no life after death, would you view your own intellect as a curse? What pleasures have you sacrificed for the sake of reason, and do you feel any resentment about that sacrifice?
?
You instinctively know you are superior to animals. But do you live in a way that honors the soul that makes you superior? When you act foolishly, do you lower yourself below the animals? This argument should make you examine your daily choices.
?
Suppose you truly believed that only this world matters. Would you stop seeking knowledge and moral excellence, since such pursuits often reduce bodily enjoyment? This argument exposes the inconsistency in living as if the soul ends at death. Are you living consistently with your belief in the soul's immortality?

Ninth Proof: Bodily Pleasures and Human Perfection
Sanusi states the premise of the ninth proof from universal reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the premise of Sanusi's ninth proof based on universal rational intuition about bodily pleasures and human perfection.

Core Idea
Less indulgence in bodily pleasures leads to greater human perfection.

Sanusi presents a proof rooted in what all rational minds acknowledge: the value of temperance. He argues that the less one pursues bodily pleasures, the more perfect one becomes. This lesson unpacks that premise and its initial evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully.
Read the Arabic once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the universal claim 'all intellects judge'.
The passage is cut off mid-sentence, but we have the core premise.
2
Identify the core principle.
Sanusi states a clear inverse relationship: less bodily pleasure = more perfection; more bodily pleasure = baseness.
3
Consider the evidence given.
The author begins to support the premise by pointing out that people naturally esteem those who abstain from bodily pleasures like eating, drinking, and sex.
The argument is incomplete, but this is the start of the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن العقول كلها حاكمة بأنه كلما كان الخوض في اللذات البدنية والخيرات الجسمية أقل، كان ذلك الإنسان أكمل . وكلما كان الخوض فيها أكثر ، كان ذلك الإنسان أخس . والدليل عليه : أن الناس إذا اعتقدوا في إنسان أنه معرض عن اللذات الجسمية ، كالأكل والشرب والوقاع ، ومعرض
The ninth proof: All intellects judge that the less one engages in bodily pleasures and physical goods, the more perfect that human is; and the more one engages in them, the baser that human is. The evidence for it is that people, when they believe of a person that he turns away from bodily pleasures such as eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse, and turns away...
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the ninth proof, stating a premise affirmed by universal rational intuition (al-ʿuqūl kulluhā ḥākima): the less a person indulges in bodily pleasures, the more perfect he is; the more indulgence, the baser. The author then offers evidence: people naturally esteem someone who abstains from bodily desires. The argument is incomplete here but clearly aims to show that human perfection lies in something beyond the body, implying the soul's survival. The text breaks off, but the intended reasoning would complete the proof.

Key Terms
العقول (al-ʿuqūl)
اللذات البدنية (al-ladhdhāt al-badaniyya)
أكمل (akmal)
أخس (akhass)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is against all bodily pleasures, deeming them inherently evil.
Correction: The argument uses the universal esteem for asceticism to show that true perfection is non-bodily; it does not deny the permissibility of lawful pleasures.

Checkpoint

B) The less one pursues them, the more perfect one is
C) They are irrelevant to human perfection

Key Takeaway
All rational minds agree that human perfection is inversely related to the pursuit of bodily pleasures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it look like for you to intentionally turn away from one physical good this week as a step toward that perfection?
?
Think of someone you deeply respect for their character. Is their perfection rooted in their restraint from physical desires or in their pursuit of them? How does that mirror or challenge your own daily choices?
?
You know this judgment to be true in your mind, yet you often act as if the opposite were true. What specific bodily pleasure are you holding onto that is preventing you from becoming more perfect? What would it cost you to let it go, and is that cost worth the gain?
